“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Comnlete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System. ■
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System - one House to
, be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
Country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless newspaper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
f'ttd follow its Hubs. The result of its powerful
influence tind its unprecedented literary and oom-
mefcial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever se< n.
The Bulletin is the only well-established A us-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the clabns of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushmai. of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly baaed.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

A Question of Taste.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“I WA3 on an island in the Solomon Group,”h®
said, “ and the only white man there on one occa-
sion. I was collecting copra for a well-known
trading-firm and had a pleasant enough time
until I fell sick of New Guinea fever. For-
tunately, I pulled through pretty quickly. As
soon as I was able to eat anything, the old woman
who had been appointed by the natives to attend
me, because she knew some English, began to
fetch me little delicacies to tempt my appetite.
One day she brought me some cooked meat in a
piece of split bamboo, which they use as a dish—
you know that, Mr. M‘Guinis.”
He turned towards a big, red-bearded jour-
nalist who had been sent to the Islands some
years before for press purposes by a Melbourne
paper.
“Ido, Dickson,” replied the red-bearded man,
with a great sigh.
“Thatmeat, gentlemen,”said Dickson, solemnly
stroking his black beard, “ was like nothing else I
ever tasted in my life.”
“I believe yon there,” observed M'Guinis,
regarding little Tapley, of the evening press, who
was uncommonly plump, with a wistful glance.
“ Well,” continued the narrator, “I got right
again rapidly. The old woman, at my special
request, brought mo the same kind of meat every
day until a day came when she brought me fish
instead. I asked her why she hadn’t brought mo
the meat. She shook her head slowly and replied,
‘No more lit’ boy. Lit’ boy all done.’”
“ Will you tako another drink, boys ?”
We shook hands—all of us except M'Guinis,
who probably wanted to talk over old times and
tastes with Dickson. We had no heart for drink-
ing after this story.
When wo got outside the hotel little Tapley, of
the evening press, said, “ You boys may say what
you like, but I’d just as soon go picnicking with
Marcus Clarke’s Gabbett as with these two fellows
we’ve left inside.”
CREEVE ROE.
A Tewkesbury (Eng.) monumental mason
advertises that he has “ splendid designs of
marble monuments just ready for the coming
season.” “The coming season” is good. In
England it would probably be the cholera or
small-pox season; in sunny Now South Wales,
the bank-audit season.
Dear Bulletin, —l am seriously thinking
of standing for an agricultural constituency at
next election. My political creed, which has been
carefully fixed up to suit the principal yearnings
of the bucolic soul, is as follows
All agricultural produce shall be carried on the railways
absolutely free.
Every farmer shall have a frontage to the main road
and another to a permanent creek.
There shall be a full moon every night.
There shall be a strong arid convenient gate every 400
yards along every road, so that the selector's daughter
and her “boy" oan lean on it conveniently while con-
versing under the aforesaid full moon.
It shall rain hard every morning between one and three.
The adjacent town shall, in every case, be the principal
one in the province, and shall never be more than two
miles distant.
Every selector shall be supplied with six or more large
dogs out of the consolidated revenue.
There shall be a large bridge over every river, creek,
rivulet, billabong, water-hole and mud-hole in the elec-
torate. ■
There shall not, under any circumstances, be more than
two roosters in any one setting of eggs, and I am strongly
opposed to bush-fires and gum trees with big tap roots.
The above are the principal features in my pro-
gramme, but others will be added as I go along.—
Yours, Titus Salt.

(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It’s an errant thought of an idle mind
That the Arch-Fiend’s fate might be
To carry death to the human kind—
Be “ Death ” to humanity.
That perhaps it may be the Arch-Fiend’s wage—
Since the wage of sin is death—
To wander for ever, age by age,
Killing with every breath.
That wherever he goes—by land or sea—
He should hear th’ expiring cry.
And list to the ceaseless liturgy
Of a people doomed to die!
That his legions ever, by night and day,
Pursuing their courses fleet,
Should swoop in their headlong flight to lay
Their spoils at his bloodstained feet!
S.A.
PALLAS.
Old American story with valuable moral:—
Theophilus Harrington, a Vtrmont judge, was a man
who loved the right and cared little for mere legal quib-
bling. “If justice controls your verdict,” he would
often say to the jury “you will not miss the law’s general
principles.” At one trial, where the possession of a farm
was in question, defendant produced a deed, to which
plaintiff’s lawyer, Daniel Chipman, objected, because it
had no seal. “But your client sold the land; was paid
for it, and signed the deed, did he not ? ” asked the judge.
“ That makes no difference,” said Chipman. “The dosd
has no seal, and cannot be put in evidence.” “Is any-
thing else the matter with the deed 7 ” asked the judge.
“ I don’t know that there is.” “Mr. Clerk," said the
judge, “give me a wafer and a three-cornered piece of
paper.” The clerk obeyed, and the judge deliberately
made and affixed the seal. “There, Brother Chipman,”
said he “ the deed is all right now. A man is not going
to be cheated out of his farm in this court when there is a
whole box of wafers on the clerk’s desk.”
The Bulletin’s Missing Word Compe-
tition No. 10 closes on Wednesday, Jan. 24, 1894.
One of the sensations of the Monson
murder trial in Scotland was a ghastly experiment
practised on three corpses. The cadavers were set
up in a position as nearly as possible resembling
that occupied, or supposed to be occupied, by
Harnbrough at the moment of his death,
and then shot at in order to decide
whether the fatal wound could have been self-
inflicted. After the bombardment, sundry
doctors, with the gruesome cooksureness which
characterises the medical expert when he
is swearing away somebody’s life, decided that
the case was obviously one of murder. As a
matter of fact, however, they couldn’t have
proved anything of the kind. The theory of the
defence was that Harnbrough had been climbing
over a fence, or scrambling through some
brushwood, and dragging his gun behind him,
after the customary fashion of the idiot who
“didn’t know it was loaded.” Therefore, if the
medical experts had climbed over the same
obstruction, each dragging a loaded gun
behind him, and each expert had shot
himself in the process, it might have been
decided whether a man could blow the side and
most of the back of his head off by his own act.
As it was, the experiment only proved that it
could be done by somebody else’s act, which every-
body knew before. Anyhow, the Monson case
has supplied a new and valuable precedent, which
may prove useful in the future. Some day, when
a man is bashed to death at a political meeting,
and the defence pleads that he really killed himself
by pounding his own head against the doorstep, the
law will hire a prize-fighter to pound with his fists
on the head of a corpse, and thus decide whether
the skull-fracture arose from outside causes or
otherwise. It will be a gratifying spectacle—a
late sitting of the court by the light of a few
flaring gas-jets; a sleepy Judge blinking on the
bench ; the medical experts standing round watch-
ing the progress of the experiment; and the
bruiser in the box with the cadaver’s staring head
in chancery, pounding away on the senseless,
shaven skull, with ink blots on the scalp to show
where the fracture ought to be. Then when the
disorganised remnant seems to have been suffi-
ciently pounded it will be removed and dissected,
and if the result is not sufficiently definite a new
corpse will be brought in, and the defence will rise
and demand that a new bruiser shall be appointed
on the ground that the previous one was a biassed
witness, and the fresh pugilist will be duly sworn
to bang the remains in the interest of the truth,
the whole truth, and nothing but the truth,
according to the good old formula, and then he
will seize upon the remains, and get them into
position, and go for them.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 6, 1894.
The Apotheosis of Premier Patterson.
J. B. Patterson, the fierce meat-man of
Castlemaine, who is now Premier of Victoria,
has been organising a new grovel. His idea
is that the people of Australasia, or rather a
few dozen politicians who are hungering
after titles and personal distinctions, and
who will profess to represent the sentiments
of the people, should send a cordial invita-
tion to the Duke of York and his bride,
inviting them to visit these provinces, and
listen to the enthusiastic whoops of “ their
future subjects.” According to the eloquent
language of the inflated Patterson, the mvita-
tion would be a “ gracious act but perhaps
it would be better to let that energetic ex-pig-
slayer speak in lus own words. A circular
letter addressed by him to the other Aus-
tralasian Premiers, in which document the
great idea was first mooted, runs thus
Would it not have a K ood effect on the whole of Aus-
tralasia just now to get our future king and queen, the
Duke of York and his you.ig wife, to piy us a visit, say
during next year? I think it would be beneficial in
every point of view. Anything that strennthens the
cords between ua and the old country strengthens our
position and importance. The visit would probably be
extremely interesting to “Young Australia,’’ with whom
it would assist the growth of patriotic and loyal feeling,
and tend to popularise our connection with that old Imd
from which we sprang. At this juncture, too, an event
like this would do much good in raising the spirits of
these communities after ihe late depression, which we
have all felt so much. The Prince has been here before,
when he came in his boyhood as a young sailor in the
Bacchante, and this may be expected to whet his interest
in revisiting the same scenes with his wife, now that he
is heir-presumptive to the throne. I would propose that
we should each send a cordially-worded telegram through
the Governor simultaneously, instructing the Agents-
General, and following up with a memorandum, which
the Governor can forward by mail. Kindly let me have
your answer as early os convenient, as the Princes
programme is probably filled for some time ahead.
• # *
Patterson’s absurdly ungrammatical sug-
gestion, it is interesting to note, has been
adopted promptly and with enthusiasm, and,
as we write, the invitation is already on
its way. It purports, of course, to be sent
iu the name of the people of Australasia,
though, as there has been no semblance of a
pretence at consulting them, the assumption
that it is their invitation is as utterly false
as are “ loyal ” assumptions in general. And
the refusal will probably be as prompt and as
enthusiastic as was the invitation. For the
Duke of York is a personage of immense
value to the British monarchy, and his life is
not to be lightly risked on the seas, or among
possibilities of infection beside the Yarra.
Since the death of the Duke of Clarence his
brother, the weakly York, is the only in-
dividual who stands between the throne and
the horrible possibility of a Fife dynasty.
If he were to die without an heir, then the
Imperial heritage must go to an awful Scotch
family with kilts and red hair on its legs,
and this prospect is too dreadful for contem-
plation. Britain has been ruled by foreigners
for so many years that the possible accession
of a British sovereign is a thing to
be avoided by every imaginable means.
Also, Britain has such a slavish reverence for
royal blood, even if it is the blood of a
German sub-monarch whose kingdom is only
a Sabbath-day's journey in circumference,
that the accession of a “subject” would
damage the royal tradition, probably beyond
repair.
* • •
Therefore, the chance of the British royal
family risking the life of its heir on any far
journeyings is just about the same as the
chance of an Australian squatter turning out
his 700-guinea prize ram to run wild in the
bush. Consequently, the knighthood which
would almost certainly fall to Butcher James
if the invitation were accepted is about as
hopeless as anything well can be, and his
circular is chiefly interesting as showing his
own lamentable state of mind. His first
idea is that a visit from the Duke of York
would help to strengthen the “cords” which
bind us to the “mother land.” Other
“ loyalists ” have babbled about the “ silken
thread ” which connects us with that remote
island, but Patterson’s metaphors are large
and hulking like himself, and suggestive
of the hangman’s rope, or of German sausage,
or of the gristle in his own bullocks. If
Patterson were a monarch his yearning
after the large and the imposing would impel
him to reign with a golden bedpost for a
sceptre—or else with a Cape-0 tway-range
tree-trunk. According to his view, the Duke
of. Yorks advent would help to raise the
spirits of the community—which simply
means that Patterson s own spirits would
be raised if he got a knighthood, and that
he mistakes himself for the community.
And there is something weird and awful
in the suggestion that Australia should hurry
up with the invitation before the Prince is
otherwise disposed of. It is reminiscent of
the old butcher days at Castlemaine, when
James B. Patterson’s customers were in-
vited to send in their orders promptly in
order to avoid disappointment. Patterson
can’t rid himself of the recollections of these
old days, therefore he wants Australasia to
send in its order promptly, lest the last
fore-quarter of the Duke of York be disposed
of before it arrives, and the sirloin should be
all gone, and there should be such a run on
the prime cuts that nothing remains.
Patterson’s invitation is, doubtless, meant
as a compliment, but the compliment comes
down in much the same w r ay that the aggres-
sive Premier used to come down on his own
blood-stained meat-block in his younger
days. His desire to grovel before the heir-
presumptive may be pleasant enough for the
heir himself, but it is decidedly unpleasant
for the present occupant of the throne. No
sovereign, however aged, cares to be cabled
to by a blatant butcher at the other side of
the world, with a reminder that she is only
mortal. But the ambitious Patterson, in
his own coarse fashion, is a far-seeing man,
and probably recognises great possibilities
in the royal visit—if it ever comes off. Next
year there will probably be another heir in
existence, and the succession to the British
crown will be provided for down to the
fourth generation, for the Guelphs are a
painfully numerous family, and the supply
of heirs never runs short. And if the Duke
of York could be induced to visit Australia
next year, that heir might be born on Aus-
tralian soil, and Butcher James, as the
originator of the visit, might hold it as god-
father at the baptismal font, and might be
the first to suggest that Britain’s un-
developed king should in future be known as
the Prince of Australia. Then the city where
the birth took place would get so far ahead
of all other Australian cities that it would
almost to a certainty be the capital of Fede-
rated Australia. Therefore, the Victorians
would set forth the great advantages of being
born at Toorak or Brighton, and New South
Wales would point out that Manly Beach is
a good place to be born at, and Queens-
landers would demand that the future king
should be born at Southport, and there would
be great confusion and danger of civil war in
the land. And, at last, as the only way out
of the difficulty, it would probably be neces-
sary for the royal visitors to take their way
to Thargomindah in the great wild west, and
let the coming monarch see the light for the
first time under the wattle and in the shade
of the ragged eucalyptus. Then, when his
great-grandmother is dust, and his grand-
father and his father have taken a short turn
each upon the throne, he could reign as
Thargomindah 1., Emperor of the Back-
blocks, and Australia would rise to great
honour thereby, and the aggressive jowl of
Butcher Patterson would be graved in im-
perishable brass and erected all over the
land.
# # #
There is one chance —a microscopic one at
the best—that the ex-meat-man’s dream may
be realised. The health of the Duke of
York, like that of the rest of his family, is
not of the best, and the idea of sending him
out to recuperate in the land where his
uncle’s wash-bill is still unpaid, might have
some attractions. When a British royalty
of any importance goes to recuperate on the
continent of Europe the process is apt to
consist too exclusively of much drinking
with foreign emperors, and to avoid this
evil the Duke of York might be despatched
to Australia for a brief season, there to be
killed by colonial beer and talked to death
by wild wattle poets. If he should come
The Bulletin can only pray that the local
anarchist may throw no bombs at him, and
that he may be allowed to go his way in
peace. Australia was made disgustingly
loyal for twenty years because a local
maniac shot the uninteresting Edinburgh,
and the country has even now hardly
recovered from the injury done to the
under part of that parsimonious dignitary.
But the chances of Patterson’s order for a
fore-quarter of royalty ever being filled are
so exceedingly remote that this aspect of the
case is hardly worth considering. The Duke
of York is not coming. Patterson’s knight-
hood, so far as it depends upon the Austra-
lian advent of Pork—we mean York—is only
a dream, and the vision of the Victorian
Premier scowling affably at a pair of royal
twins as he holds, them at the baptismal font
is a vision and nothing more.
» « «
Therefore, about forty tons of unnecessary
food will remain unconsumed, and four
thousand hogsheads of needless liquor will
remain undrunk, and many clumsy, though
well-meaning, citizens will not make a
hideous exhibition of themselves in their
effort to dance lightly on an unaccustomed
toe, and much apoplectic feeding and much
wild cheering by fat, red capitalists with
grease and crumbs in their beards, will be
altogether left out. And, as further conse-
quences, many incoherent and hiccupy
speeches about the silken bonds will con-
tinue to be undelivered, and many frantic
poems beginning with “Great offshoot of a
royal race,” and similar unsupported state-
ments, will be unwritten and unsung,
and vast quantities of agitated slobber
which would otherwise afflict the daily press
will be a lost affliction. Also, Thargomindah
I. will be missing from the roll of British
monarchs, like those other three shadow
kings, James 111., Charles 111. and Henry
IX., who moulder under a tombstone at
Home. Therefore, The Bulletin suggests
that it would be a delicate attention to erect
a marble mausoleum at the dreary town out-
back, with an inscription upon it setting
forth that it is sacred to the memory of that
British king who might have been born in
the metropolis of Federated Australia under
the auspices of Sir J. B. Patterson, but for
the fact that his parents didn’t come, in con-
sequence of which he was born somewhere
else, and, as a further result, Thargomindah
never became the capital of Australia, and
that country is not Federated, and Patter-
son continues to be plain Butcher Patterson
to the end of his discordant days.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Twang.
Chief Justice Madden has dared to attend a
girls’-school Speech Day, and tell the angels
that their speech is disfigured by a “colonial
twang.” He besought them to pronounce the
English vowels as they are intended, and to
keep their little noses out of the matter as
much as possible. Whereupon the Argus
started a solemn enquiry into the why and
wherefore of twang in this country, and came
to the conclusion that it is begotten of mental
and physical laziness. Ghastly nonsense is
lavished upon the subject of “ colonial twang.”
Practically there is no difference between the
dialect of the London loafer, who leans against
a wall and spits, and the pronunciation of the
Southern larrikin, who does ditto. But in this
country the nasal loafer is more generally in
evidence, his voice is more heard, and his
accent infests the land instead of being localised.
The early English convicts, mostly from Lon-
don, brought it over with them. Early Aus-
tralian parents were too busy, and generally too
uneducated, to notice that their offspring had
caught the complaint, and said “ kike ” for
“cake,” and “gripes” for* “grapes.” In
England the desire to imitate the twang is
knocked out of children at boarding-school.
The twang is considered “ very low indeed ” by
people who drop their h’s freely. Austra-
lian girls are instinctively correct as regards
the old, preposterous Anglo-Saxon aspirate,
whilst their twang, bred amidst the courts
and alleys of New Babylon, is also
very pronounced. If the thing is to be
eradicated, the reformers must start upon
State schools at once, for every year brings its
thousands of recruits to the twang brigade. At
present there is no effort made to raise the
standard of State school accent, nor are the
masters in general aware that it is a terrible
thing to hear the youngsters reading. They
read nearly as nasally themselves. The twang
is everywhere—in Victoria, at any rate—ring-
ing in one’s ears. If it remains on familiar
terms with society for a few years longer, it
■will become the accepted pronunciation of the
country and pass as “ good form.” But whether
the “ colonial twang ” dies out of Australian
mouths, or grows and strengthens and is im-
proved, on the American system, the fact will
remain that it was never at the beginning any-
thing better than the twang of cockney vul-
garity. We imported it, long before rabbits,
sparrows, snails, and other British nuisances
were grafted upon our budding civilisation. /
The Japs in Torres Straits.
The Brisbane and Sydney daily press has sud-
denly become violently aware that Japanese
are crowding the whites out of Thursday
Island. The curse has long been notoriously
apparent in Torres Straits, and has by no
means been abated since a Japanese syndicate
won £27,000 net in a big Southern racing
sweep. Japanese own, perhaps, the finest fleet
of boats to be seen in the Straits, and it is no
uncommon sight to see these boats at work in
British waters with the Japanese flag flying at
the peak ! These boats employ only Japs,
They are not registered in Japanese names,
and it is a legal question whether the Q.
Government could not, if it chose, forfeit and
sell them. While men down South need to be
warned against working their way up to
Thursday Island, in the hope of there earning
good wages. Except a rarely fortunate one,
the white man who lands moneyless on
Thursday Island drops into the quagmire of
bottomless loaferdorn. Most “ stations,” ashore
and afloat, refuse to employ white men, or only
accept them so far as is absolutely necessary.
The constant instreaming of Japs keeps the
market in full supply of crews. The heathen
usually arrives unable to speak one word of
English; but he is shipped as “seaman” all
the same—a fact that should be interesting to
Australian marine assurance offices that accept
risks on boats the crews of which cannot under-
stand the orders given. The Jap “ sea-
man ” is on the articles as receiving stan-
dard wages; but, as a rule, he receives
much less. He “learns English” during
his first term of engagement —usually six
months. As to the female question: White
women who trade in their own charms are
peremptorily ordered oft' the Island. Japanese
harlots ply their calling undisturbed. More :
they often devote their savings towards pur-
chasing boats for their male friends. All this
is ancient history, furrowing itself more deeply
into the life of Thursday Island every « year.
But, since good dog Towzer has asked the Slip-
Press to give one yap in unison with his bark,
it has been thought worthy of resurrection. It
would be interesting to know when John
Douglas —unquestionably a man of mental
nobility and integrity—a gentleman of honor
who has given signal proof of his contempt for
social caste—sent in his first report on this
subject to any Q, Government, and why his
reports have been merely pigeonholed,
“ToDied Away.”
A little while ago Premier Patterson, of Vic-
toria, announced, in his own frank and butcher-
like fashion, that the Treasury had no more
money to “fool away” on providing work for
the unemployed. Therefore the unemployed
were to shift for themselves, and the bounceable
James B. washed his hands of all responsibility.
Considering the piteous state to which Mel-
bourne, in particular, is reduced, the statement
seemed rather unfeeling, but most people
assumed that Patterson was acting under the
pressure of hard necessity, and that his mode
of expression was not meant for inhumanity,
but was merely the result of deficient education
and natural coarseness. Melbourne, ever since
the boom, has been about the most hunger-
strikeu and abjectly wretched city south of the
line, and death from star station —a thing hither-
to practically unheard-of in Australian cities—
has begun to make its ghastly appearance
at intervals in the daily newspaper re-
ports. Still, if the Treasury is absolutely
empty, as Patterson represented it to be,
then there is no immediate remedy for these
horrors, so far as the Government is concerned.
Patterson explained all this with the friendly
air of a man who brains a calf, and the country
accepted it in the same spirit. Yet only a few
days after his announcement that he had no
money to fool away in granting relief to the
hungry and the helpless he conceived the great
idea of inviting the Duke of York to Australia,
and filling the country with banquets and
champagne and all manner of glittering orgies
in his honour. Therefore, Patterson can
obviously find either money or credit, provided
it is to be spent in grovel instead of food, and
(incidentally) in scrambling after a title for
himself instead of furnishing work for the
hungry masses. The coarse and bowelless in-
humanity of the ex-butcher here stands out
conspicuously —in fact, it is difficult to
know whether his inhumanity or his
coarseness is the more conspicuous. To
Patterson the relief of the worst distress ever
known in Australia is a “ fooling away” of
public money, while the unauthorised and
inevitable expenditure of thousands in shows
and banquets, which will only weary the royal
recipient and make him wish that Patterson
were in the nether hell, is a lofty and com-
mendable proceeding. Yet, among all the
Governments of Australasia there arose not one
Minister with courage enough to tell this
offensive politician of his brutality and his
wormy degradation. Truly democrats are
wonderfully scarce in Australia.
Duncan Gillies, the newly-appointed Vic.
Agent-General, is probably the least en-
lightened public man in Australia. He knows
little about this country, and has forgotten
anything he may ever have known concerning
the country he came from. His history is re-
markable for lack of variety, Duncan having
been one of the most monotonous persons that
ever lived upon the revenue. Thirty or forty
years ago the Ballarat diggers returned him to
Parliament on account of his peculiar inability
to find gold an} 7 where else. With the charit-
able idea of “ doing something for talker
Gillies,” who could do no good for himself with
a pick, they sent him up to the House.
And to the House he has clung like
a barnacle. The miserable little political
world of Victoria is the only world that
Duncan is a man of. There he has moved—
or, rather, sat—during what ought to have
been the productive portion of his lifetime,
absorbing the petty secrets of v political
“generalship,” acquiring an unwholesome mass
of official experience, and cultivating the art of
doing nothing with an air of importance. He has
lived only to sit in Parliament, and lias sat in


Parliament to live. Under his premiership the
state of Victoria turned rotten and the finances
went raving mad, to the subsequent surprise
of Gillies, whose little mind was full of gas
and tactics all the time. Quite unconsciously
he was a curse to the Constitution, by reason
of his ghastly ignorance of things outside the
House. There is but one side to Duncan
Gillies’s character —one narrow, thin side,
which consists entirely of front. As an up-
holder of Victorian “prestige” in London it
would, no doubt, he difficult to beat him. He
is specially built and trained for any position
that has no real purpose beyond the adjoining
salary.
Grandfather Parkes, at latest advices, was
going to Westralia to deliver speeches to the
inhabitants. When he returns from W.A. he
intends to stump Maoriland. Now that Higin-
botham— hats off!—is dead and Judas Grif-
fith has vanished from the political stage,
Parkes feels more hopeful than ever that he
will be the first Premier of federated Australia,
and when he shakes his hoary mane over the
seven provinces of Australasia he feels that he
is practically stumping his future electorate.
But a man of Parkes’ character and ante-
cedents must never he permitted to engineer
himself into the George WashiNgton-slhp of
Australia. Should he ever dare to pose
as an aspirant for what: will amount to the
Presidentship, a full, true and exact
history of him, personal and political,
written with a knife, printed in vitriol, and
signed with the name of the author, should be
sent to every man and woman on every electoral
roll in Australasia, and to the editor of every
paper in the English-speaking world. So far,
the Presidential posturings of this shock-
headed old political greaser don’t signify as
much as a row of pins, and may be smiled at
accordingly ; hut should he venture at time of
action to aspire to the chiefship of our nation
there are several newspapers and scores of
thousands of men and women ready to
demonstrate that Australia has not yet
sank so low as to he dependent for
a figure - head upon any person of his
calibre. In the Federal Convention he cut a
despicable figure and was heartily desp sed by
all his colleagues, even by some who were joints
in his tail; and ten years after he has departed
and has been followed to Heaven or elsewhere
by Ids miserable claqueurs he will he as dead,
historically, as is Governor Phillip’s butler, if
Gov. Phillip ever had a butler. Parkes, as
energetic an advertiser as the late pill-man,
Holloway, has never yet given people an
opportunity of sitting down in a cool place and
asking “ What has he done?” What has he
done ?

The first note of discord has been heard from
“ New Australia.” Hitherto the news lias
dealt .exclusively with oranges and blossoms,
with dark-eyed seuors playing softly upon
guitars, and moonlight dances on the deck as
the steamer ploughed its way up the shimmering
Parana river, and, in a general way, with
soft breathings of love among the bowers and
the ethereal beauty ami romance of things in
general. But now it is briefly reported that
eight common, large-hooted Australians, with
nopoetry orguitarororange-hlossom about them,
have risen up and quitted the settlement in
protest against “ the injustice of the adminis-
tration ” (which, say Lane & Co., cut off their
grog-supply), and have telegraphed to the nextlot
of argonauts to carefully stay away. This is
probably the first result of Lane being made
Dictator. In New Australia everything was
to he decided by popular vote, but instead the
Paraguayan Government made Lane sole
magistrate, chief of police, and the whole
system of local government in himself, and so
the dream faded. Lane, in fact, seems to have
been appointed absolute boss - tyrant, and,
however wisely he may use his power, the fact
that he is boss-tyrant knocks one beautiful vision
on the head.
On Saturday night the jury in the Speight-
Syme libel case returned a general verdict for
Mr. Speight with £lOO damages, bub were sent
hack to make a separate deliverance on each of
the numerous counts; however, that £lOO
represented the aggregate opinion of the jury on
Saturday. It seems a pity that the verdict
wasn’t accompanied by the jury’s reasons for
arriving at it. The Age undoubtedly wrecked
Speight’s character to the very utmost of
its ability. Therefore tne jury either in-
tends to convey that Speight had only £lOO
worth of character to he damaged, or that
the Age, when it is cursing its hardest with the
loud pedal down, can only do £lOO worth of
damage altogether; or else that Speight’s
business character was exceedingly had, but that
the Age represented it as being worse, to the
extent of £lOO worth of libel, than it really
was. Anyhow, the jury intended to convey that
Speight should receive £lOO and his costs from
Syme for injury received. And, seeing that the
recovery of that £lOO will probably cost
Speight £lO,OOO, over and above the costs
allowed by the court, the case is chiefly
valuable as another advt, for the iniquity of
the law. That grand old Oriental device,
according to which an intricate case used to be
settled by making the plaintiff and defendant
dive in deep water—the verdict going to the
party who came up last or who never came up
at all—has been improved on in Australia. For
in this country neither party comes up again,
and neither gets a verdict. The verdict goes to
the lawyers on the hank.
The Victorian Cabinet has decided that
Frances Knorr, the hahy-murderer of Mel-
bourne, shall be executed in the good old way,
and the rope is being soaped and the gallows
carefully overhauled accordingly. It is diffi-
cult to see what other decision a Cabinet, with
a solid faith in capital punishment, could have
arrived at. The crime of which Frances
Knorr was convicted was one which pre-
sented no single redeeming feature. It was bald,
unvarnished murder, perpetrated not from sud-
den passion, or drink, or jealousy, or revenge, or
under any form of provocation whatever, but
done in cold blood and from the meanest
motives. It was, in short, professional murder
done for 305., or thereabouts. The only pos-
sible reason why Frances Knorr should not
be executed is the fact that she is a woman,
and in a country which supports the gallows as
a permanent institution there is no more cause
why a woman should not he executed (or
flogged) than there is why a man should not be
executed or flogged under the same set of cir-
cumstances. The rope fits her neck quite as
well as it does that of a male assassin, and the
formal inquest on a woman with a broken neck
is- no more horrible than the inquest on a man
with a similar dislocation ; and, barring the
fact that her skirts may possibly get in the way
of the drop and that her hair needs to he cut
short in order to facilitate the arrange-
ment of the noose, her sex makes no visible
difference. The plea that capital punish-
ment is altogether brutal, disgusting, and
unnecessary may he a logical reason why
Frances Knorr should not be hanged, but the
plea that there is any special horror about the
execution of a woman, because she is a woman,
is a mere spineless sentiment, and the Cabinet
of ex-Butcher Patterson evidently realises the
fact. The matter was formally decided last
Friday, but it was really decided the day that
a damp judge passed sentence and commended
the criminal’s soul to God according to the
good old platitude of the law. The decision
was written in legible characters on Patter*
son’s aggressive underjaw, and anyone who
knew that portentous bone would have recog-
nised it from the very first.

The hundred days —Speight v. Syme.
Sympathy.—A speedy means of getting
gossip.
A refreshing cable item is that informing
us of the sale of the Crown jewels of France, the
proceeds to be devoted to an old-age pension fund.
Ex-Gov. Carington has taken a house in
Grosvenor-st., London, occupied by Mountmor-
ganaire D’Arcy before he blossomed out into
Grosvenor-square, and smart society.
Said a clergyman to Mrs. Trollope, the
novelist: “ Last Sabbath, seven persons were out
yachting. The boat capsized—three were provi-
dentially saved.” Mrs. Trollope: “And I
suppose four were providentially drowned.”
Sydney is getting quite tired of its
stipendiary magistrates. With hardly an excep-
tion, they are utterly unfit for their positions, and
should be removed from the bench in favour of
quite a different class of men.
Jenkin Coles, of S.A., has been created
a K.C.M.G. Jenkin is an ex-policeman, and
for many years conscientiously “ ran in ” the
festive drunk. Otherwise he is chiefly notable for
being Jenkin, which is almost as unhappy a name
as Jabez.
Mayor Manning, of Sydney, has been
made a knight, which will be pleasant news for
the other enhandled local Manning. The newly-
ennobbled politician will probably make a com-
paratively unobjectionable K.C.M.G. In public
he eats with his fork, and his long and beautiful
legs are calculated to do justice to the Order.
Hayter, the Victorian statist, classes the
travelling in a first-class carriage on a second-class
ticket as an “artificial” crime. There is a good
deal of Victorian political sentiment about Mr.
Hayter’s definition. Land-boomers, according to
“representative” people, were merely artificial
swindlers, the public debt is but an artificial
calamity, everything dishonest in Smellbourne is
only an artificial result of a superior moral
rectitude.
In ’B9, before the bush unions were amal-
gamated, and when the burning discussion of the
time was whether the A.S.U. and the Q.S.U.
should exchange tickets, an argument took place
on the subject at Tongo, on the Paroo, the princi-
pal debaters being old Kerry Dick and Bogan.
(All back-country men will remember Bogan.)
This is how old Kerry Dick snuffed out all
opponents: “Will yez tell me this, now? Sup-
posin’ Oi’m shearin’ in New Sout’ Wales, an’ yure
shearin’ in Kunesland ; shure, yure not shearin’
in New Sout’ Wales, an’ Oi’m not shearin’ in
Kunesland. Phat Oi wants to know is—will Oi
be ree-cog-nised by the blanky Unions? That
puts yure pipe out.” And, for some reason or
other, it did.
Marriage was soon a failure in the case of
a woman, who started matrimony, in Melbourne,
last December 12. She left domestic service to
enjoy all the comforts of a homo with a labourer
possessed of £3O capital and good prospects. For
two days, during which she cost her husband only
“a penny for hairpins,” in addition to her keep,
the bridal pair were presumably very, very happy.
On the third day the bridegroom notified his
better half that he was going away, never to
return, nor had he returned at latest dates. A
Bench, on being applied to, said “it would be a
pity to part the young couple,” so they issued a
warrant for the arrest of the missing partner and
remanded the case for a fortnight. This magis-
terial faith in the re-uniting properties of the law
is most affecting. If a husband repudiates his
bride after two days’ trial, and tells her not to
worry him further, the difficulty, it seems, can be
overcome by a warrant. The great object of the
Court is to make married couples live happily
together—an obvious impossibility in cases where
the husband remains absent. Therefore they send
a policeman to bring back the sheep that was lost
and domestic felicity is restored.
A London paper :
Paraguay is essentially a land of women, for the men
have been swept off by civil wars and revolution, especi-
ally by the wars of Lopez, and the women are anything
but a nice lot. Of course, now and again, one comes
across a very magnificent specimen of the Guarani girl,
tall and stately, serene of countenance, with skin like
brown satin, piercing black eyes, heavy black hair falling
in braids below her waist, and figure well-formed and
graceful; but the picture is always spoilt by the big cigar
which she is sure to be chewing and sucking at all the
time. From a very early age, the women of Paraguay all
smoke big cigars, and the natural result is that their
teeth are blackened very soon by the nicotine, and their
breath is far from savoury. Besides, even the handsomest
of Guarani girls “ goes off ” in the most painful manner
in a very short time. Before she has well
reached middle age, this dusky Diana will be
as hideous a specimen of decayed humanity as her
mother; and a Guarani mother is always an awful old
hag—scrawny, blear-eyed, and wrinkled as a dried fig.
The majority of the women are, however, not Dianas at
all. They are ugly in face, shrill in tongue, sad and
miserable in appearance, and, moreover, splay-footed,
owing to their constant disuse of shoes and stockings.
Add to this that they are always sucking at one of the big
coarse cigars of the country, or chewing the end of one,
and the picture is complete. In one thing, however, the
women of Paraguay excel. Their figures are wonderful,
and their carriage is Juno-like. This is mainly due to
their habit of carrying packages on their heads, which
strengthens the spine as in the case of Hindoo women.
Such adepts are the Paraguayan girls at this that they
will carry an empty water-jug poised at a rakish angle on
the head, and go down the st reet stopping to gaze at any-
thing of interest, or to talk to acquaintances without for
a moment endangering the safety of their load. The
women of the upper classes are mostly half-bred
Spaniards. They are usually far from handsome, but are
very amorous and very jealous. They are also lazy, and
not particular about ablutions. Their idea of pleasure is
to eat sweets and swing in hammocks. They are devoted
to dancing, and dance with an energy perfectly astound-
ing by contrast with their usual laziness. They are born
intriguers, and have played an important part in the
secret history of all the civil disturbances of the country.
A Requiem.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The sun was bathed in the heaving sea ;
The sea, in the glow of the sun ;
And there came, from the glorious harmony,
Three voices, blended in one—
Voices that, passing, sadly told
Of the ceasing of things to be,
In the wane of the year, the sunset gold, ,
And the ebb of the sobbing sea.
Soft western winds, through briar and cleft,
Delivered the Year’s “Good-bye ; ”
And the wistful East grew grey, bereft
Of the Year condemned to die.
The sands lay bare as the sun sank down—
The sun that killed the Year;
And the whole world wept, in funereal gown,
As the night-pall draped the bier.
Gloomily sounded the muffled deep,
Within its hollow caves ;
And I heard a world of voices weep
Behind the swirling waves—
Weep for the life, for the love, for the light;
Weep for the hope, beside,
That had died with the Year, and gone out, that
night,
Away on the ebb of the tide.
Thou Power, that best in the Caves of Death !
Thou Slave-god, lying in thrall!
Of life, the life ; of breath, the breath ;
Thou Mystery, hid from all !
That shap’st from decay, in charnel-den,
By a subtle alchemy,
The forest-tree and the brains of men,
Ay, all that comes to be
Say, is’t so, with the souls of the sons of men,
As it is with earth’s grosser mould?
Shall hope, love, life, be shapen again
From the sere and dead and cold ?
The Slave-god is silent. Though he may hark,
He will not answer me ;
And the Year is dead, and the West is dark,
And eternally moans the sea.
Paeroa, M.L.
ELWIN EDWARDS.
In Norway, before a widower can re-wed,
a certain portion of his property must be settled
on the issue of the former marriage.
“ All the so-called drink-cures are im-
postures. There is no cure for inebriety but total
abstinence,” says B. W. Richardson. Amen.
Adelaide Advertiser on the recent local
tragedy:—
The sickly sentimentality which induces people to gloat
over scenes of horror and death drew crowds to the
thoroughfare in which the butchery was committed, and
all day long corrupt curiosity caused men and women to
gape and stare at the commonplace shop in which most of
the bodies lay.
Then follow seven columns of long-drawn-out
“ blood,” “ brains,” “ gaping wounds,” and
“bloody weapons.” And. the ’Tiser wood cuts !
The murderer little knew what he was inflicting
on the poor Adelaide public !
Although the Bill to abolish Supreme
Court holidays was ejected from New South
Wales Assembly last session, it seems certain
that the “long vacation” is not going to be
so long nor so complete as it used to be. The
Supreme Court offices are now to be kept open
right through vacation, and much business will be
taken in various courts during January. Also, it
is said that the judicial Joshuas are not going to
make the sun of litigation stand still for eight
weeks, but, on the contrary, will allow time to run
in matters of pleading during at least a part of
vacation. Which changes will suit the struggling
junior who can’t get away for a long tour, but will
much “ nark ” the seniors, who will have to limit
their excursions.
An English paper reports the discovery
that if peat moss is heated up to a certain degree
with diluted sulphuric acid and afterwards fer-
mented with yeast, excellent whisky is the result.
Apropos of the heat and the general thirst, this
seems a fitting season tor an item on strange
drinks. In one of the Ellice Group of islands the
natives had, and perhaps still have, a wholesome
family drink brewed by winding fathoms of coir
cinnet around pigs’ snouts till the animals are ready
to burst with suffocation, and then cutting their
throats and mixing the hot blood with the pulp of
mashed-up young coeoanuts. Also, in one district of
New Guinea, according to a well-known missionary,
the decease of the head of the family is made the
occasion for much quiet festivity. The body is
placed upon a platform in the house, said plat-
form being built for the occasion, and in about a
week or eight days it is generally reported to be
sufficiently mellow. A committee of the mourning
relatives then sits around with drinking vessels in
their hands, and chant the virtues of the swollen
departed. Then the deceased’s nearest relation
rises up and—but on the whole it seems advisable
to change the subject just about here.
Apropos the whales disporting outside
Sydney Heads recently. Two years ago, one of
Macgregor’s (Tas.) whaling captains, having cut
the blubber from a whale, was about to cast the
rest adrift, when there came alongside two Hobart
fishermen —“Portuguee Joe” and his mate, an
African negro. The Portuguese begged to be
given the carcase, so that they might tow it
ashore, and make what they could of it. “All
right,” said the skipper, with the generosity of a
satisfied exploiter who knew the blubber business
to its omega. Joe, having got leviathan’s frame-
work on to the beach, began to search for
ambergris, which drug was quoted at that time in
the current price-lists at somewhere about =Bl3
per oz. He found 174 lb. Many people inter-
viewed him and wanted to give him from <£sooo
to .£9OOO for the lot; but the man understood the
luck of his find, and got a friendly and dis-
interested Hobart resident to place him in direct
communication with the best London brokers.
Meanwhile, the ambergis was lodged with a bank
—which was presently served with an injunction,
on behalf of the Macgregor firm, to restrain the
sale of the precious prize pending a discus-
sion re ownership. But these legal fireworks
fizzled out, and the ambergis is still being
gradually realised in London, the two fishermen
having already received several thousands of
pounds apiece. By the way, a Dunedin (M.L.)
chemist once gave a few hundreds for a lump of
ambergris, which he afterwards sold for .£9OOO.
That chemist knew “too much, yet not quite
enough.” At the then market rates, he should
have got £50,000 for it, instead of £9OOO. Amber-
gris is a substance that has baffled scientific defini-
tion. It is found floating on the sea, and in the
intestinal canal of the cachalot, or spermaceti-
yielding whale. It appears to have its origin in
some of the while’s meat-diet, and has been
regarded by some us being in the nature of biliary
calculi.

Were The Bulletin asked for the recipe
of “Beauty and the Beast,” at Sydney Lyceum,
it would answer: “Take 987,645 tons of King
Beetle splendour, striped paint, pink legs, lime-
light, frocks and female loveliness, and flavour
piquantly with one comedian disguised principally
in a rug-strap.” Lauri is that comedian—without
him the whole glorious splodge of dazzle would
probably give you ocular dyspepsia. In a word,
he is the olive of the show, and the other wags,
hired to tone down the sentiment and relieve the
loveliness of the thing, merely clear the_ course for
him. ’ The test of the true comedian is when he
is confronted by a limp and doddering book that
doesn’t even fib the scenery, and is expected to
tickle the groundlings with wheezes as old as
Abraham. Then art steps in when author fails —
and “author” fails like a sandbank in “Beauty
and the Beast.”
* * •
Miss Nellie Stewart is personally very
happily cast as Beauty, and although her style is
much too refined for even the most gentle form of
variety business, still a real artist always fits into
the puzzle somehow. Her song of “ Molly and I
and the Baby” will be whistled everywhere, and
the very Prenchy “Marguerite” song is another
huge success—the fanciful dance included. By
the way, the management might enlarge the Blue
Couch in the Beast’s Palace —the human intellect
totters at the mere thought of what would happen
if Beauty had the nightmare and plunged about
much. Florence Young is far and away too
girlish for the Prince, but she sings her songs
acceptably and looks extremely neat. Polly
Emery and Elsie Cameron, as the comic sisters,
are amusing while they last. Their first duet,
“O, Mister Porter !” is the funniest duo in the
’mime, but the “ fat ” is scantily served out to the
twain, and beyond sporting loud colours and fat,
false cheeks, they are not a side-splitting experi-
ence all through. Vernon has rather a hoarse
explanatory part as a monkey major-domo,
Lissant’s legs act very well as the Beast.
« » «
The butterfly Bartho is the spirit of the
spectacular part of the ’mime, for whenever there
is a great rush of elaborately-clad people and a
ranging of forces for dazzling ballet-spectacles,
Bartho, of the swansdown floatsomeness, is sure
to find a clear patch to disport herself. In the
extravagantly-dressed procession of nations at
Chicago (which fell a little fiat on Boxing Night,
because there was no allegorical Australia to set
the gods going) Mdlle. B. gives a series of national
dances, which are not only correctly dressed and
stepped, but are done with national feeling. At
the same time, Bartho in ballet-costume as the
white butterfly of the first act is undoubtedly
Bartho at her best. Lucy Cobb gives a serpentine
dance, made gaudy by a plaid reflection —like the
spirit of the Duff tartan wrestling with the lime-
light-man. “Beauty and the Beast” is a very
overwhelming spectacle, and if W. & M. try to
outshine it on some future occasion the Arch-
angels will probably send down a celestial law-
yer’s letter to say that Paradise is copyright. The
usual harlequinade, arranged by George Lauri,
plays the audience out of the house.
*» * *
Another courageous management has arisen
at Sydney Imperial Opera House—coming in like
a lion, to go out, probably, like a very badly shorn
lamb if the old blight doesn’t lift for the new
year. An energetic opera 00. commenced a comic
opera season in that haunt of theatrical frosts on
Boxing- Night, opening in the “ Royal Middy.”
As the versatile and much-mixed middy, Miss
' Oolborne-Baber proves herself the king-bolt of the
show. Miss Baber, besides looking pretty, acts
piquantly and tirelessly, and though her speaking
voice is borrowed from various sources her singing
voice is bright and clever. “Willie Walsho”as
the giddy king, controls his strong tenor so as to
till the theatre without raising the roof, and is
burdened with a part in which the victim has to
stand in an attitude of profound attention while
various people bully-rag him from the centre. One
pleasant thing about the gigantic Walshe is that
you never fear when the heroine faints backwards
on top of him that she is going to bowl him over.
• • •
Farley, as a lymphatic Don with plenty of
“fat,” renews a success of Emelie Melville’s
traditions; and Miss Edith Moore, as the Queen,
manages her voice and her train much better
than the only other Queen The Bulletin ever
saw. That monarch, to whom this paper now
figuratively kisses its hand, had a long train
of Guv’ment blanket working out between her
large, flat feet, and she was asking for ’bacca in
a voice not a bit like Miss Moore’s. Mr. Mor-
rison, as Mungo, is the wag of the undertaking;
and Harry Craig, as Don Domingo, might be a
great deal worse. The “Royal Middy” isn’t
staged to bankrupt the management, but the
dresses are well done.
“Jack the Giant Killer,” at Sydney Her
Majesty’s, is running strong and well for its
second week, and the howl of the agitated infants
when Joe Tolano writhes across in the iron grip
of a giant whose head is lost in the flies is highly
complimentary to the management. Also the
comic characters seem to have a deeper sense of
their responsibilities. “A funny man” is—or
ought to be—a sort of snowball of wit and humour
-—you should never know when he’s finished grow-
ing—the mechanical witticisms of pantomime
authors are never good enough to travel
on. Apropos of “Jack” —the Xmas-keeping
compositor inferred last week that the writer
thought the harlequinade more than atoned for
the transformation, which is an error of the
deepest dye. Brunton’s “Birth of the New Year”'
is a glittering vision of unearthly beauty that
maltej one even forgive the clown when he is pick-
ing eggs out of the pantaloon, as if that knock-
knee’d annual were an incubator.
** * *
“ Charley’s Aunt,” which holds possession
of the long-deserted “ Cri.,” is a little tale whoso
merits undoubtedly are high—a short and simple
story of the multifarious woes which fall upon the
spirit of a man in woman’s clothes. The man a
British lord is ;he is short and dull and fat; his
legs abbreviated are, and bis mind is brief and
squat. The tale begins at Oxford with a dinner
laid for five, but one member of the party fails
somehow to arrive; and one comes uninvited with
a red and dr inky nose, and the fizz has been em-
bezzled by the man in woman’s clothes. Four
members of the party are very deep in love ; one’s
in mourning for a husband who has lately gone
above; one’s in drink, one’s in the army—at least,
we’re led so to suppose—and one, as already men-
tioned, is disguised in women’s clothes. This per-
son’s manners, so to speak, are much behind the
age, for he seriously proceedeth to undress upon
the stage; but when, without his frock, he
bounds upon his native heath, we find, to our
relief, he’s got his pants on underneath. If he
hadn’t, what might happen it’s impossible to say,
for he’d probably be naked—in a general sort of
way.
• • *
The people all belong unto the aristo-
cracce, and are distinguished by the elegance of
the caste of Vere de V. And their habits are
conspicuous by a mild and soft repose—save for
the frenzied antics of the man in woman’s clothes.
And, excepting for the fact that, ’mid the
evening dews and damp, one who props his devious
footsteps with a mouldy cotton gamp, and
whose legs suggest some crooked place where
the spiral corkscrew grows, makes love to
the aforesaid man in the aforesaid clothes.
And a military person with a figure long and lank
desires to hug him lovingly upon a grassy bank.
Still, the comedy in general is filling to the soul,
and, only for the absence of Uncle Oattermole, it
might be even better than that comedy of yore
where the boneless curate leaveth bis goloshes on
the floor. The company is uneven, and one im-
ported dude should be assailed with eggs by an
indignant multitude. But otherwise the story,
like a sparkling river, goes, and a thrilling in-
stitution is that man in woman’s clothes.
* *■ * *
Messrs. Gourlay and Walton’s pantomime
at Sydney Royal improves considerably on a more
enlarged acquaintance. Some of the beauties,
which were formerly lost in the confusion, begin
to emerge and more definitely assert themselves,
and the loveliness of the transformation-scene
grows upon the audience, and the procession of
English kings and queens, which can’t get any
better owing to the badness of the material, at
least manages not to get any worse. Still, if this
item could be cut out and replaced by a grand
march of famous murderers, each carrying bis un-
buried victim in his arms, or by a procession of
fraudulent bankers, or by a great review of ghosts,
devils, absconding bankrupts, prominent drunk-
ards, hangmen and bushrangers, it would be a
relief. Australia doesn’t take so profound an
interest in British kings and queens as the Royal
management seems to imagine.
* * * *
Coogee Aquarium the establishment
where the Blue Groper resides —has a holiday
Srogrammeof about 240 items, includingPunchand
udy, snakeobarming, performing fleas, palmistry,
a minstrel show, concert, farce, ventriloquists,
and a string band. That infant phenomenon,
Phyllis Perrier, is on next Sunday’s programme.
This year’s pantomime at Melb. Princess’s
is the ghost of last year’s ditto at Sydney Lyceum,
retrirnmed and altered in sundry places. Its
furtive attempts to tell the story of Red Riding
Hood constitute a gross insult to childhood. A
man in black satin smalls, a swallow-tailed scarlet
coat, and the face of a prehistoric hairy animal, is
supposed to be a fox in league with the wolf. The
wolf is also a hopeless case, whilst the grand-
mother he threatens to devour is left out of the
cast. Seeing that some trouble is taken to make a
purely idiotic narrative, it would probably have
been worth while to turn out a faint suggestion of
the nursery legend. Serious stupidity is a sheer
waste of space in any form of entertainment.
Elsewhere the show is more entertaining than
the average Xmas medley. A Sports Pro-
cession, including animated cricket-bats and
other implements of recreation, is a pleas-
ing, although a slightly tedious, novelty,
depending for humour on the occasional collapse
of a cricket-bat or the eccentric behaviour
of a billiard-ball, which will, perchance, lose its
way and require a fairy’s kindly aid. Caiecedo,
the wire-dancer, is another unduly elaborated
item, outlasting the astonishment be creates.
Caiecedo is, properly speaking, a bounder on the
wire, using it as a spring in turning somersaults
and landing upon one foot if necessary. He puts
himself into a pair of jack-boots and repeats the
whole of his business with the same neatness as
before. A wonderful but monotonous performer,
not half as amusing as Wainwratta, the droll dude
who (with flesh-coloured tights under his dress-
suit) used to stroll along the wire shedding gar-
ments one at a time until at last he stood shiver-
ing in his shirt. There is nothing of the mad wag
about Caiecedo.
* » *
Dame Courtneidge forms the leading
comic element and chief reminder that a panto-
mime is happening. He is an elderly female with
an eagle eye that takes in the situation at a
glance. He conducts a riotous infant school, sings
the only songs that catch on, and seems to have
definite ideas as to what he is about. Some of the
other men are sadly wanting in this particular.
Miss Violet Varley, in scarlet cloak, is prettily
paired off with Jennie Lee, but, whereas Violet
makes the most of some feeble ballads, Jennie’s
lively ditties get lost in her upper register. Miss
Ethel Haydon warbles concerning the roses which
bloom, a subject that has fairly earned a
rest. Miss Rosie Nott puts in a bright
and festive appearance, but she, like several
other nice visions, is seldom called upon
to express her feelings. Miss Mary Weir’s mystic
whirl and D’Argo’s fascinating pas are thankfully
received ; also the chorus effects, arranged on
operatic principles, abound in sweetness and lime-
light, and the public attendance for the past week
may be described as splendid,
** » »
At the little Gaiety Theatre they have
■ventured upon a pantomime burlesque. James
Wilkingon and Miss Amy Johns head a small
company in H. J. Byron’s witty old version of
“Cinderella,” and a miniature transformation
scene, likewise a glittering fairy coach with ponies
attached, are displayed with reckless indifference
to the cost of such luxuries.
** * »
Maggie Moore is the beginning, end, and
most of the middle portion of “Sinbad the Sailor,”
at Melbourne Royal. For some three or four
years past Miss Maggie has been steadily grow-
ing younger. Just now she is so exceptionally
youthful and full of infectious “go” that the
pantomime, after being handicapped by a poor
start, is making a bold effort to get level with her.
The show is urged on, lured on, and pulled on by
Maggie Moore, whenever it needs a friend.
Premier Patterson’s infantile scheme for manu-
facturing “loyalty” was an unexpected stroke of
luck for the Royal, inasmuch as its chief scenic suc-
cess is a panorama of the Duke of York’s wedding,
painted by Grist from pictures in English illus-
trated papers. The subject, of course, is harrow-
ing to every well-regulated mind, but the
panorama is pretty, and Patterson has advertised
it at Government expense. Miss Isabel Webster’s
voice is another charming feature, some variety
business chips in opportunely, and Martin Hagan,
who met with gallery opposition on Boxing Night,
seems to do very well in a good part. Leumane
gets an encore for requesting Australia to Ad-
vance. Also here, as at the Princess’, there are
many precocious children, and hordes of banner-
bearing supernumeraries addicted to coming and
going in the time-honoured pantomime style.
m* * *
Dan Barry, who has often talked of giving
his travelling melodramas a little metropolitan
experience, produced “Ransom,” at Melbourne
Alexandra, last Saturday week. The Haleck is a
proverbially ill-fated house, but if the present
management cannot drag affairs right along to a
successful issue fate must be very sick indeed.
At less than half of ordinary prices, Barry stages
a capable, smart-looking company in a somewhat
lavish manner. Two or three of the backgrounds
are cheerful bits of painting. Also, a fair-sized
orchestra performs lively music in tune, and the
general appearance of the venture is businesslike.
It is surprising to find so much care bestowed on
a mere shilling shocker. “ Ransom ”is a furious
absurdity in five acts, introducing two corpses
and a good deal of live _ horse. Although
the story has been located in “ bygone bush-
ranging days ” it admits of modern reference to
Carbine’s Cup, and the patriotism of Miss Dora
Mostyn, as an angelic bush urchin, is quite up to
date. Flourishing a stockwhip, Miss Dora (the
Jennie Lee of the piece) declares that Australian
boxers, Australian trees, cricketers, sheep, miners,
butter, frozen meat and sporting men (!!) are each
and all of them the noblest works of God. The
average British hero, raving about his country’s
Flag, is a modest, retiring person by comparison
with this golden-haired boy. Apart from a balder-
dash plot and clap-traps for the gallery “Ransom”
is a pleasant affair. The acting all round is so far
from being barnstormy that the company might
raise their voices without doing any damage to the
roof. Their quiet naturalism is rather too quiet for
the Haleck’s acoustic properties. J. P. Oathcart
and Prank Cates, who have been specially engaged
for villainy business, both die dreadful deaths. Two
Eromising young men are J. G. Patten and D. L.
lalziel, well balanced by Miss Eugenie Duggan
and Miss Ida Gresham. Miss Duggan has learnt
to moderate her enthusiasm since she Julieted at
the Theatre Royal, a while back, and Miss Gresham
requires only a little more confidence and voioe-
traming to make her an excellent ingenue. Dan
Barry and Miss Sydney walk through superflu-
ous parts in an easy manner ; the rest of the cast
being mostly bandits, who pin the aoe of clubs
over their victim’s heart for pistol - practice.
“Ransom” has confirmed first night opinions by
drawing big houses all through Xmas week, and
it is still flourishing.

One night during his triumphant “Silver
King” season at Melbourne Princess’ Walter
Bentley was suddenly taken so very ill that he
forgot his lines and sat speechless on a garden
seat. They gave him his cue several times to no
purpose, and finally Wilfrid Denver made a hur-
ried departure, leaving the rest of the scene to
take care of itself. Miss Henrietta Watson then
came to the rescue, accompanied by little Cissie.
“Which way did the kind gentleman go?” said
Miss Watson to Oissie, who, as a matter of fact,
had never seen the kind gent., because he col-
lapsed before the interview with his cheeild should
have come off. “ That way, mamma,” said Cissie,
the adroit fibber, pointing to the back of the
stage, whereas Walter had really made his exit
at the o.p. side. “Go and bring him back!”
shouted Miss Watson to the aged Jaikes. For-
tunately the play makes out that Silver King
catches the train for London at this moment, and
before his next “call” came, Bentley was suffi-
ciently recovered to resume operations.
** * *
The late Henry Pettitt was essentially a
playwright. He devised melodramatic plots and
filled in the fustian dialogue, looking to his colla-
borates to draw character sketches and complete
a stage story which the scenic artist, assisted by a
clever mechanist, might make a presentable job
of. In his early days Pettitt partnered Paul
Merritt in dozens of blood-and-thunder dramas of
the old “transpontine” school, suitable for audi-
ences who mostly sat in their shirt-sleeves and
brought bags of nuts with them. The villains of
these plays had to be paid a little extra on account
of the awful risks they ran, after trying to choke
the heroine in the third act. It was not unusual
for a stone ginger-beer bottle to catch the villain
napping and lay him up for a fortnight.

Melb. Age, in an article regretting the financial non-
success of the late Italian opera season, refers to the
musical enthusiasm of Milan, where, “ a few da.\s prior to
the first performance of Verdi’s ‘ Falstaff,’ one found the
whole city in a state of almost feverish excitement about
the new opera; peop.e thought of nothing else, talked of
nothing else.” Which is all-very sweet and soulful from
a musical gusher’s point of view. But whilst Italy thinks
of nothing but new operas, the country grows more
poverty-stricken every day, and pretty soon the national
mind wall be concentrated upon the question of how to
live happily on nothing a year.

The following epistle has been received at this office..
It is signed “Aim6e Moore Dear Editor,—Perhaps
you will allow me as the person most concerned to settle-
the questions which have lately agitated your corres-
pondents. Some people have insinuated that I am nob.
equal to Bernhardt, but then they were not judges, just,
as those who suggested that my beauty is not more
transcendental than Mary Anderson’s were bound to see-
crooked. And to those who suggest, I am too slight II
would say that I have an elbow people come miles to see,.
In the same way those persons who whisper that my,
wardrobe is not so complete as that of Mrs, Potter must,
know that the couple of millions I recently realised at,
my own benefit I am now rapidly expending on beautiful!
costumes to appear in at other people’s benefits. ’Tis true:
in this wise 1 am not amassing a huge fortune, but the:
deep and wide-spread gratitude and vast stage experience
which I receive repay me amply. -Some of my friends;
suggest that I am not as great as'Siddons, but these
persons mostly consist of disappointed Romeos to whom:
I have no time to play Juliet. In a word, dear editor, I!
am the finest actress in the English-speaking—or any.
other speaking —world. I say it, and surely I ought tm
know."

The term “Her Majesty's servants” is still (or was;
until quite recently) applied to the companies at Drury
Lane and Covent Garden theatres. The shows at those-
two old-established houses enjoy the meaningless privilege:
of being authorised by Royal Letters Patent, or, in some-
other equally vague and musty manner, they are supposed
to be different from ordinary theatrical performances.
Also, a couple of British grenadiers are (or were) told off
every evening to act as sentinels at the principal entrance,
and fight for Her Majesty’s servants if necessary.
You must blame “Tay Pay" O’Connor, of London
Star, if this is a lie. Beatrice Battenberg is very much
up to date as regards music-hall songs, and she adores
Chevalier’s cosier ditties. She plays selections for “Ma”
after dinner, and the eldest Battenberg boy is permitted
to put his granny to sleep chortling “ Knocked ’em in the
Old Kent Road.”
Lord Sholto Douglas, youngest son of the (newly re-
married) Marquis of Queensberry, is a veritable chip of
the old block. Recently he had a fair go-in at a music-
hall at Sandgate, and it took three policemen to look him
up. He had been pouring liquor from the dress-circle
upon the heads of the people below, and was fined £2.
