846 Macquarie-streeb North, Sydney,
January 3, 1894,
My Dear Moorabinda—
A Block spectacle in Sydney on New Year
Saturday morning was a couple of —I pause here
for a badly-missing word ; but, let us say, skipping
infants—who made the whole street stand aghast.
One was 16 and well-developed and wore a pink
dress cut d la “My Sweetheart,” showing nine-
tenths of her stockings and surmounted by a pink
sun-bonnet —but Elsie Cameron in the Panto-
mime is an ordinary schoolgirl to the other one.
Her dress was high above her boots, too—a
white dress peppered with black spots like un-
cooked plum-duff and fitting loosely over an Elsie
Caiqeron figure above the waist-belt. This infan-
tile array culminated in a white-frilled sun-bonnet
from which peered forth the visage of a woman of
over 20 ! The spectacle the two presented (they
had somebody representing “Nursey” along) was
absolutely paralysing. It looked as if the apos-
trophised mamma in “ Make me a child again ”
had come back and inserted her elderly daughter
into a sun-bonnet as per request. Also, they re-
minded me of the giant babes at Her Majesty’s
out for an airing. Even the newsboys’ breath
was taken away by the spectacle, and there wasn’t
a man on the Block who, so to speak, didn’t know
those girls’ legs by heart in half-an-hour.
p suppose we owe the variety-stage another for
this wondrous sight. An old-young girl goes and
sees the actress who she knows is over forty
looking something sooner than eleven in open-
work socks and pinafore, and she conceives a
stupid idea of carrying out the wrinkle on the
Block! And some people do it successfully.
Gracie Whiteford, for instance, looked like a little
girl, even in the street, where she always dressed
in short frocks. A couple of variety sisters whom
you see occasionally in the street keep up the
baby game rather cleverly—but such tricks are
only possible to the round - featured, slender
woman. The charmer of fourteen-stun must never
buy a sunbonnet and a smock frock, and hope to
be admired as a fine child by any responsible
critic.
I am told that that other legal youth of baritone
voice, well-known in Melbourne and Sydney
“ best society,” who has thrown his brief-bag sky-
high and will shortly bid for footlight fame,
will make his maiden professional effort in the
land of wooden hams. It is also said that the
open-mouthed admiration of his gifts expressed
by the late Fred Leslie is responsible for the mis-
chief. I only hope he won’t have to transpose
the Lyceum ’mime chant into “ 0 Mr. Leslie,
what have you done for me?” after he’s once
adopted grease-paint.
The departure of the squadron from Sydney the
other Friday was a solemn affair. Very few ad-
mirers rushed out to the Heads to see the iron-
clads steam steadily over the heaving tide,
seeking out a cool spot to summer in—Hobart
this time. A faithful few, egged on by the mer-
curial Major-General and Mrs. Hutton, were
gathered together in the military reserve at South
Head to watch the farewell signals ; but the im-
pressiveness of the ocean tableau was spoilt by a
boat-load of Ohinamen, who placidly fished all the
time the warships were slowly filing past. Six
Chinamen in the middle-distance would ruin an
epic.
There are whole oceans of spectacle in “Beauty
and the Beast” at Sydney Lyceum, and the person
who is obliged, for reasons of space, to boil the
whole panorama down into a mere simmer of
approbation, should plunge at once into admira-
tion points over Nellie Stewart’s Marguerite dress,
in which Beauty cuts the sort of piquant figure
that gets on to the cover of a French society print.
The gown is in Marguerite colours—gold and
white the jaunty satin skirt, trimmed with
festoons of white lace and fans of golden tinsel,
the seams outlined in jewelled gimp; gold tinsel
in the hair, golden slippers and a golden gamp.
But the new thing about the dress is that it is
lined in black, and when lifted reveals pink-blue
petticoats. A horrible idea strikes the spectator
when those petticoats are first revealed—that
Nellie is going to peel down to them in full view
of the stalls, but she doesn’t. A grey silk smock
and Greenaway cap make Beauty look fourteen
in the first act, when she is surrounded by gold,
green and red infants, who reminded me, by the
way, of the fate of the youngsters who teased a
dyer, with the result that their victim caught a
handful of them one day, dipped them, clothes
and all, into the dye-vats and then let them loose
a covey of red, blue and purple bantlings—as a
warning for the rest.
A ribbon gown, long, razor-strop-looking tags
of cream satin faked with gold and separating
over four-o’clock-ooloured tights; a smart pink
hunting dress and a web-like, golden-starred
gauze over cream tights, were Nellie Stewart’s
most gorgeous other gowns. The Prince was in
coffee-colour taffeta and terra-cotta satin glitter-
ing with jewels and topped by a pink satin hat;
but the neat Miss Young appeared most kissable
in the grey satin bicycle suit and cap which she
carried very smartly. An inherently-neat woman
always looks unhappy with her hat hanging on
her ear, which is the principal objection to Miss
Young in the sea-gull suit and the wedding
glories inevitable in the last act. May Pollard’s
Fairy Queen, in pink-and-grey silk and gauze,
was a well-dressed Titania, even if the colours
rather reminded you of a rosella parrot; and
Lauri, in a travelling-rug, wore a red necktie
round his bared gullet, looking for all the world
as if the gifted oomique had severed his jugular.
Talking of the principals’ dresses Nellie
Stewart hasn’t an understudy for Beauty, so, if
anything happens to Nellie the management will be
reduced to the necessity of giving ’Amlet without
the Ghost, so to speak. W. and M. wouldn’t go
to the expense of duplicating the heroine’s radiant
raiment, and as the star won’t let any perspiring
imitator wear her frocks —not if she knows it —
the situation remains as stated. But it strikes me
that a really shrewd manager would make it a
point in the signed articles that the leading lady
temporarily forfeits her purple and fine linen to
an understudy whenever she cannot appear her-
self. The average actress would go on for 52
weeks if she felt there was a possible r-r-r-rival
waiting to jump her low-cut corsage and her fan-
tail frock. But women everywhere are spikey
about their wardrobes. The culminating point
of feminine friendship is when Darnonida lends
Pythiana her best spring bonnet.
The eggshell Mdlle Bartho, like the red-haired
Sarah, “no speakee Ingleese,” and it is a terrible
affliction for the local jeunesse doree to try and
make tender insinuations per medium of the
sheep - dog which accompanies Mademoiselle,
and superintends her in five languages.
Where is the bold, bad man who could possibly
carry out his nefarious schemes through
an interpreter and that interpreter the
maiden’s chaperone to boot? Bartho, herself, has
the advantage of being able to talk with her
whole graceful form and twinkling “ silver pedes-
tals,” as the Easterns call a woman’s feet —bub
the feeble Johnny, in wide pants and a glass eye,
whose ideas, even in English, only come to the
surface after long pauses of mental grappling—
cannot hope to express his absorbing passion by
chopping meaningly about on his large tan feet.
So there is no hope but the polygot Cerberus.
By the way, Bartho wears nary a di’mond, I
notice.
The most bewitching costume into which Bartho
inserted herself in the ’mime was her white tulle
ballet-frock with gold-spangled corsage and two
perky little butterfly wings jutting out at exactly
the right moment from her dimpled vertebrae. A
yellow silk and black lace turn-out with black and
gold Bolero jacket worn in the Spanish dance was
characteristic, and so was a Russian get-up of pale-
blue satin designed in gold, and crimson velvet coat
edged with brown fur, a smart cap and top boots
and spurs ; also, a blue-and-white satin sailor suit,
in which she condescended to do a very British-
sailor-like hornpipe in the fastest time on record,
comes into the same category—but I really must
protest against those blue-satin breeches. Any
garment that conceals the twinkling tibia, so to
speak, of the symmetrical Bartho is obviously
over-doing its earthly mission. By the way, on
the first time, one of Bartho’s silvery spurs shot
off into the orchestra. The reserved stalls never
have any luck.
In a whirl of life-like butterflies, brown bats,
demons, fairies, pages and ladies draped in Judson
hues of spangled gauze over fleshings long-drawn-
out, one pounces upon the stately upholstery of
the procession of Nations at Sydney Lyceum—
every yard straight from ’Ome —and you can’t
help thinking how lucky it is that all the powers
revel in National colours that harmonise so well
when they come together. I like the_ Indian
dresses best —the filmy diaphonies flashing with
fire-fly jewels, and the bizarre topping off —the
upper half of an elephant’s head in ele-
phant grey. France was a mingling cf airy
tri-colour silks and wreaths of laurel; Ger-
many (one looks at the standard-bearer, by the
way, for the most frills) in white satin and black
plumes, and a long white velvet cloak designed
in black “tri-antelopes,” apparently. Turkey
was barbaric in green, cream, crimson and gold—
all the colours of a swell narghili—and came on
led by a very well-supported officer in dark-blue
and a fez. Britain’s cloak was the British
standard over a large white satin lady who waved
the flag without-an-allnsion-to-which-no-melo-
drama-is-complete, and was backed up by several
pale-green silk and emerald-clad sylphs copiously
trimmed with ich dien feathers who looked highly
artistic. Indeed, the most monotonous thing
about the March of Nations at Chicago was the
sumptuousness of the dressing. If Spam had
only been trying to hide a patch on its trousers
or Austria seemed conscious of odd boots, the
audience wouldn’t have felt so sun-struck. A
shower of gold is sometimes as unhealthy for the
man without an umbrella as a shower of bricks.
The feature—from my point of view—of the
“Royal Middy,” at Sydney Opera House, was
Edward Farley’s neck. From a gorgeous satin
collar, left loose a long way down —it undulated
like the frontal aspect of a wax doll, and must
have made half the ladies on the stage green with
envy. Miss Colbourne Baber wore a very modest
suit of blue and crimson satin and gold embroidery
as the middy (a prima-donna who teaches young
ladies how to warble in her disengaged moments
has to be particular), but the inexpressibles were
a little too voluminous, giving the effect of a mer-
maiden’s tail. However, Miss Baber’s pink silk
dress became her admirably. The athletic Willie
Walshe was gorgeous in rainbow silk and lace as
the King, and the best dress Miss Moore wore as
the Queen was a white satin robe with a court
train of blue velvet edged with ermine. At least,
that’s how I sized up the regal garment, but I
have no documentary evidence to prove that it
was ermine. Still there is no more incongruity
in calling a yard or so of Tom-cat “regal fur”
than in alluding to some cotton-back chorus-girl
as an empress. It’s all “ staige ” glamour, I
suppose.
Somebody at ’Ome has been interviewing a
wonderful spot-stroke billiardist who said, inter
alia, that women are taking to the game much
more than they used to do, and that many would
be lured on to the green cloth if they could only
be taught by one of their own sex. “ I hope my
little girl will go on playing,” said the man of
cues, “for I see in it a handsome income for her.
Here is a new occupation for women which is
not overcrowded.”
Bub surely billiards can never become a she-
game—there are too many awful possibilities about
where the next stroke is to be reached from to
make a feminine diversion. Imagine a fat woman
sprawling half over the table (as we used to see
Roberts on ladies’ day) poking at a ball in the
exact centre ! No short-legged charmer would
play billiards decently without a step-ladder
either —no matter how high her heels were. There
is no need for a sharp, nerveful woman to take to
billiards. She could earn quite as much at scien-
tific card-playing, especially if she were pretty
and had shapely hands, also a diamond ring or
two and an unsuspected genius for solo-whist.

cher, Q.C., of Sydney, to Mr. Jay, who hails from
England.
The feature of the Rignold-Huenerbein sacred
concert, at Sydney Her Majesty’s on Xmas night,
was certainly the fiddling of M, Henri Staell—a
nervo-fibrous person who stands on the stage like
a man getting his sea-legs and who reminds you
of Riccardi starved down to nine “ stun,” Mons.
Staell (lately from Paris) plays with everything
but fire. In a tempestuous finale he fades away
like a Cup favourite at the finish, but in quick,
bird-like movements and dainty pizzicati he is
indeed a ourly-haired fiddler, besides always play-
ing with appropriate expression. Kowalski
accompanied the hero in concerto. By the way,
when Kowalski is effacing himself instrumentally
he moves his hand on the piano like a cat crossing
a wet patch.
Apropos of music, a full-length picture o*
Madame Amy Sherwin, the Tasmanian Nightin"
gale, is published in a ’Ome society paper*
_“ Madame ’’Sherwin is represented standing up,
in a do-or-die attitude, with a dagger in her back-
hair and a white feather fan, and, in a column of
praise appended to the picture, you learn that she
lives at St. John’s Wood, in a house crowded with
“ mementoes.”
On the word of a leading actress at present
drawing the enchanted multitude at an Australian
theatre, I have it that the front-row stalls in—to
put it expansively—Australia are not now what
they were in the dear days lang syne. The indict-
ment is that the front-row is no gentleman. If
the performer near the footlights isn’t to their
liking, they simply converse affably with one
another, laugh and chat with careless gaiety until
somebody comes on who pleases them, when they
listen patronisingly, with a re-assuring smirk.
However, I have watched a typical front stall
soaring out in the intervals to get a breath of air,
and I can hardly imagine anybody but a chorus-
girl taking any of them seriously, even if they
began to snore with their mouths open in the
middle of an aria. For they are obviously the
legal prey of the girl with the kohl-soaked eye-
balls, the double-barrelled wink, and the brief,
gauzy garment who carries a banner in the front
row, and if they wither like flowers that are wait-
ing for the rain during exits, I think the principals
should be very patient with them.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
“The Bulletin’s” Missing Word
Competition, No. 9.
The above competition closed with 1884 sub-
scribers, making a total of <£94 4s. There were
seven successful guesses, and the dividend is con-
sequently £l3 9s. 2d. Two speculative families,
which sent in several dozen coupons, gathered in
no less than five out of the seven prizes. The
missing word was “Thanksgiving.” Most of the
competitors, however, pinned theirfaith to “Jubi-
lation,” and there was also a considerable run
upon “Joy,” “Hilarity,” and “Exultation.”
One erratic theorist speculated his shilling on
“Buckjumping,” and another festive person re-
garded “Boozing” as a word that would be in
keeping with the season. The following are the
names and addresses of the winners ;

Holiday fact from Melbourne. Scene:
Bourke-street restaurant on Boxing Day, 2 p.m.
Stranger, from out-back : “Say, waiter, can I get
such a thing as a chop in this place?” Waiter
stares, gasps, reels, and falls dead.
The swank of the Melb. militia officers is
simply disgusting. About town of a night, you
see a pack of ninnies in scarlet shell jackets and
little Scotch caps, switching their legs. Every-
thing is done, with the utmost impolicy, to widen
the distance between officers and men, part of the
scheme being that a lieutenant’s outfit costs .£3O.

Mayor Manning, of Sydney, knighted,
and not millionaire Sydney Burdekin! Mr.
Manning’s bauble, is, of course, a social, not a
political decoration. Burdekin’s vigorous mayoral
and other appeals to the stomachs of the distant
great should have touched their hearts; but
Manning has probably reached a still more vulner-
able point, their purses, for he has long been, and
still is, the trusted financial representative in
Sydney of such estates as those of Rosebery and
Sherbrooke. Mayor Manning’s public farewell
oration to Gov. Jersey is an historical piece of
adulation. Somebody should look it up and have
it pasted in every State school as a model for the
good little boy who hopes to become a knight.
At a recent Bundaberg (Q.) Ministerial
“guzzle,” Attorney-General Byrnes, responding
for “ The Ministry,” spoke on the coloured labour
sugar industry question. “The late Govt, had
rescinded many acts of legislation erroneously
carried out in the past. . . . But a revolution
of feeling may come, and ‘ we ’ have endeavoured
to place the roots of this industry so deep into
the soil that no Government put into power by
a temporary wane of feeling can possibly uproot
it.” A. J. Thynne, M.L.C., then arose and
assured his audience (mostly planters and their
“ tobies ”): “ That in view of revulsion of feeling,
we are now bringing into force a system of elec-
tion to the Second Chamber that will provide a
check for hasty legislation. I am one of the
youngest members of that House, but I am look-
ing on and learning.” Let Democratic Australia
“look cn and learn ’’what elective Second Cham-
bers are meant to do. In vino veritas /
The Adelaide Hbvenink Journal starts
its narrative of the Oxley murders with half-a-
column of big, black headlines about the toma-
hawk and other horrid details. Then it girds up
its bloodstained loins and wades in to the extent
of seven columns. A few Cobbloquent extracts :
For awful unexpectedness, unpremeditatedness, dia-
bolical execution, and for fearful results, the tragedy
which occurred in Adelaide on Christmas morning has no
equal in the annals of crime in South Australia, or, per-
haps, in Australia. . . . Oxley came out in a night-
shirt and a pair of pants, but whether he had any boots
on was not known. He was evidently in a depressed
condition. ... He was a happy lad of ten years
old. The right side of the head lay exposed, and a
sheet covered the body. One foul blow with the back
of the tomahawk crashed the skull, spattered the room
with blood, scattered the child’s brains on the floor, and
left no trace of his features free from blood and brains
which streamed all over the pillow-case. The little
fellow’s hands lay peacefully on his chest. . . . Going
through the door the man would see his wife calmly
sleeping with her hands on her bosom, her legs slightly
drawn up, and the bedclothes thrown off on account of
the heat of the night. . . . Then the axe fell
again . . . rendering the head a shapeless mass. The
brains streamed out, blood flooded the place, and an
artery threw the red fluid far up the wall. . , . One
little innocent rested near the edge of the bed . . the
brains protruded and the force knocked her out of the bed
on to the floor. Her sister . . . got the blow on the
right side, her skull being also opened and the brains ex-
posed. He himself was left amid a scene of wreck to
survey his diabolical w’ork. . . . His life-blood dripped
down to the floor mingled with those of his daughters.
It splashed on to the floor as the clocks in the house
ticked out the seconds of his life. ... In two rooms
the life-giving fluid had drenched walls and floors with a
shower ... an unshapely mass of brains, clotted hair
and crushed bone . . . so crushed was the skull it
was difficult to distinguish the pillow from the head
. . . “ Mind the blood and brains,” courteously said
the constable ... It smelt of blood . . . His
naked feet pattering through the warm blood
his life oozed through the gash in his neck ... a red
bath of blood . . . blood . . . brains . , .
gore . . . crushed bones ... he saw a girl on
the floor scratching herself . . . red blotches . .
brains . . . protruding brains . . . shattered
brains . . . tragedy . . . unexampled horror

. . . gory tomahawk . . . brains, &o.

The law of social evolution ; The survival
of the fattest.
In London there are now lady insurance
canvassers galore, also lady dentists. Like-
wise, a society has been started the members of
which will call at your house and shave you o’
mornings, or dress your wife’s hair if you happen
not to be a bachelor. This new development is
likely to cause much joy among black-mailing
lawyers;

Yet another portrait of Parkes is on view
in Sydney. It is more Carlylesque than even its
latest predecessor. Those unacquainted with
Parkes might mistake the picture for a represen-
tation of Moses as he gazed upon the Almighty.
Ex-Gov. Onslow’s elocution is declared by
Rosebery to be “ so pleasant and so sweet as to be
rarely equalled.” Which, if true, goes to show
that residence in Maoriland may teach even a peer
to speak without betraying the drawl and twang
of British aristocracy.
Reporter John Rider once missed an
important case, before Judge Redmond Barry, in
Chambers. He coolly interviewed the Judge,
who gave him the whole report, and asked him to
take a glass of wine. A proud man will do such
a thing—others won’t.
There is a delicious story current re the
conversational ambush whereby Parkes, when in
Adelaide recently, ridded himself of an intrusive
clerical bore. Like most Parkesian anecdotes, it
is unprintable; but, as every N.S Wales M.P.
knows it and will tell his wife, the yarn is guar-
anteed wide circulation.

The other day, an Australian barmaid
exhibited six tumblers, each having a large
segment bitten out. “That is from Mr. Howl-
man’s last spree. Mr. Howlman is a member of
Parliament and a lawyer,” said she. (Of course.)
ALMOST A CALAMITY.
B OATMAN: “You see this ’ere spot as we’re a-passing over ? Well, about
a year ago a friend of mine ’ad ’is ’ead took off by a shark
just ’ere ! ”
PASSENGER: “My word! Lucky thing your friend got off without
losing his whole body!”
As an afterthought, the lady added : “ When we
wouldn’t give Mr. Howlman any more tumblers
to bite, he took up some bottles and bit the necks
off them.”

Dear Bulletin,— -Did ifc ever strike you,
as a shorthand-writer, that “British” and
“brutish” are written with the same brachy-
graph ? Same as “ lawyer” and “ liar,” and a few
other pairs of synonyms.—Yonrs, Dipso. P.S. —
Ditto “Parkes” and “perks,” “Dibbs” and
“ dubious,” “ Reid ” and “ rude,” “ Haynes ” and
“hence,” “ Bruce Smith ” and “brass smooth,”
and lots more.
The charming Signora Del Torre is en-
gaged to a gentleman (unprofessional) in Italy,
where Giraud, by the way, has a wife and two
children preparing a welcome home for him.
Erasca and Roussal, the two outsiders of the late
operatic company, sailed away from Australia a
week before the others. Erasca is likely to become
famous in his own country for a knowledge of
larrikin lingo. For the ladies of the chorus this
dreadful funny male had one set form of greet-
ing : —“Rye-buck and dicken to yow !”

There is at least one exception to the rule
laid down that Australian knights have invariably
fallen from democratic grace through wifely per-
suasion. Thomas Elder, G.0.M.G., of Adelaide,
is a bachelor of between 70 and 80 years standing.
On the other hand, had not Mr. Way, the S. A.
Chief Justice, been free from matrimonial shackles
it is probable that long ere this the brand of Im-
perialism would have been placed upon his brow.
Colonel Boyle Travers Finniss, the first
Premier of S.A., under Constitutional Govern-
ment, died in Adelaide on Christmas Eve, aged
86. He came to the province in 1836 as assistant-
surveyor, and helped Colonel Light to survey the
city of Adelaide. So completely forgotten was he
when he died that his funeral only attracted
mourners enough to fill four vehicles. And, yet,
once —from Dec. 20, 1854, to June 8, 1855—he was
Administrator of the Government!
At Tonga, recently, a discharged officer
used indecent and insulting language to the king,
at whom he fired three ineffectual shots. Next
day, however, ho called at the palace and apolo-
gised, and the king having accepted the apology
the incident closed. Tonga isn’t much of a
kingdom, and its potentate is a very small digni-
tary, but there are the elements of a man and a
brother about the king, small or large, who will
accept an apology after having been missed three
times with a gun.
Archbishop Murphy, of Hobart, had a
desolate Xmas. While he was at Ballarat in re
the consecration of his priestly brother Delaney,
some ruthless blackguard raided the archepiscopal
stye and laid sacrilegious hands on one of the two
pigs—which they were fattening agin the 25th
December, that due honour might be paid to the
Star of Bethlehem. The robber slew the sacred
animal on the spot, and left naught behind him
save some bootmarks and a big clot of pigly gore.
This outrage has made the blood of Tasmania run
cold, Murphy’s pig having gone, whose pig is
safe ?
Premier Dibbs’ tendency to retail Scrip-
tural variabilia is said to be the outcome of a
literary and oratorical ambition. When much
younger his style in speech and writing was
adversely criticised, and Stuart (afterwards Pre-
mier) recommended a study of the Bible for the
simplicity, purity, and eloquence of its diction.
That recommendation Dibbs followed awhile, and
the bizarre jumbles of Scriptural phrasing and
allusion to which N.S.W. Parliament' and public
are now so frequently treated result from the
application of a secular mind to religious records
in pursuit of effective diction.
Some years ago, when those distinguished
Radical politicians, Gordon and Cockburn, were
on a visit to Sydney, they stayed at the Reform
Club. Their bedroom was only divided by a par-
tition from the room occupied that night by the
late “Jack” Robertson. The Adelaide visitors
promptly cleared out of the Reform Club next
morning. They had not slept one wink, for old
“Jack” Robertson, the whole night through, had
never ceased swearing in his dreams at the blanky
blank dashy dash miserable little “ Cabbage Gar-
den.” Honest in his hates, as in his likings, the
ancient had to curse the “ Cabbage Garden ” even
in his sleep !

Fellows.— On 9th December, Charles Fellowes,
aged 60 years, well-known as manager of various
mines in N.S. Wales; he was cowardly and delibe-
rately shot d' ad whilst amusing the public, beloved
brother to Mr. T. and Mr. A. Fellowes, of Charters
Towers, and nephew to the late Chief Justice Fel-
lowes, of Victoria, leaving a loving wife and six chil-
dren to mourn their great loss. May the assassin be
rewarded for his cowardly deed.

GEORGK HIGINBOTHAM, DEMOCRAT,
Died, 31st. December, 1893.
One year, and Mem’ry, too, is dead,
Except for feeble pulsing where
Sick sentiment would fain revere
His name, ere it p’raps, too, be fled.
Then be eternal curse on Death,
On Death that ever curses all,
And holds Slave-Life in tyrant’s thrall
By mortgage bonds with seals of breath !
“ Too good to live 1 ” O basest lie 1
In this ill-throngfed, cruel world
Where gems are few, his soul impearl’d
Were, O sad Truth, too good to die.
Melb. Paul Mell.

POLITICAL
POIONTS.
What has become of the projected Sydney
Daily Post ?
Premier Nelson (Q.) has also gone on a
pilgrimage to Maoriland —with the intention of
studying what to avoid.
At the Isis scrub (Q.), certain free kanakas
lately struck against a wages-reduction, and their
places were instantly supplied by a gang of mean
whites.
Dibbs, cursing the “colonial party,” per
Westminster Gazette, says the “colonies” don’t
want meddlers. Is Dibbs opposed to imported
Governors ?
Ebenezer Ward, M.L.C., succeeds Baker
as leader of the National Ass party in S.A. Legis-
lative Council. Ward is a born leader—of any
ass party.
A large number of N.S.W. Civil servants
have only to thank the bankrupt condition of the
Superannuation Fund for thei r continuance in
their situations.
S.A. revenue for last half-year of ’93—
£1; 103,172, an increase (over last half of ’92) of
£47,826; but receipts fall short of the Treasury
estimate by £203,365.
Cabled that a shipment of Australian eggs
sent to England per Britannia arrived in rotten
condition. The N.S.W. elections are near. The
shipment should be returned instanter.
Premier Seddon lends “his enthusiastic
support” to Jingo B. Patterson’s proposal that
the Australian Governments unite in inviting
York and May to Australia. Seddon has lost his
Ballance.
Country Gentleman (Eng.) says that
Baron Oarington lost the vice-royalty of
by his former stupid public speeches abou c
“ Australia for the Australians, and Ireland for
the Irish.”
Henry Parkes says that his life has been
‘comparatively wasted.” No; “superlatively
wasted.” And that’s not the worst in this
particular matter. Parkes has contrived to also
waste some 40 years of the national life of N.S.
Wales.
W. B. Melville, Protectionist, Democrat,
&c., and formerly private secretary to N.S.W.
Premier Dibbs, now runs the Landowner, which
opposes payment of members and is the organ of
the N.S.W. Landowners’ League—whatever that
may be.
One Alderman Grose, candidate for Mar-
rickville (N.S.W.) at next election, boasts the
possession of a complete cure for poverty, but
will not reveal his secret till election time. £3OO
a year parliamentary salary is one complete cure
for poverty.
Premier Patterson says that the only way
to put the affairs of Victoria straight again is
to live on porridge and the Proverbs of Solomon.
It is Patterson’s misfortune that he looks a good
deal more like a man who lives on raw meat and
the Song of Solomon.
There is some doubt whether the approach-
ing S.A. Legislative Council elections will be
fought out on “progressive” or “direct” land-
tax lines. The progressive party is very strong in
the country, but in the city the direct taxers in-
clude the Labour-party.
The South Carolina liquor law continues
to work well. The licensed victuallers are bought
clean out, at fair market prices, and the State is
the sole grog-dealer. Real experts continue in the
trade, as salaried men, their interest being to sell
as little liquor ais possible.
Parkes is “a-lashin’ of his tail.” There
is one plumbless sea between the old fox and his
hated rival, Dibbs. Dibbs has committed one
crime for which there can be no forgiveness, no
mercy, either in time or in eternity. He has been
photographed with the Prince of Wales—and has,
so says rumour, derisively sent Parkes a copy of
the picture.
Thankfull Willmetfc is the name of the
Mcllwraithian candidate for Townsville (Q.), in
the election to fill the parliamentary seat made
vacant by the death of G. Burns. Thankf ull has been
Mayor of Townsville, and is in the stationery line.
As he is opposed by Anthony Ogden, whom
Burps only beat by one vote, he will probably re-
main stationary.

The friendly javelin is more poisonous than the
foe’s claymore.

and May: “It would tend to enhance the im-
fortance of Australia in the eyes of the world.”
f they should come “ the volume of production
and business will be so increased that all Aus-

0. A. Lee’s adjournment coup is being
put forward by the political friends of that
N.S.W. legislator as ample qualification for a
portfolio in the first Freetrade administration.
The Mines is mentioned. To the question—what
does Lee know about mines?—the answer is
promptly given—what did Abigail and attorney
Slattery know about mines ?
Henry George cheers an Australian Single-
taxer by assuring him that “our time is surely
coming.” It is ominous that the only definite
item of fact in the same letter is that “ Moxhara
—Tom Johnson’s partner,” has given “ £5OOO for
backing Single-tax members of Congress.” At
Current U.S. rates that sum ought to induce
several members and a lot of voters.
Despite the huge franchise advantages
given by the Westralian electoral law to Fat Man
and his barnacles, it is thought that the next
elections (about May) will give the overshadowing
“ Six Families ” a pronounced set-back. Hardly
one electorate in the province will be uncontested.
The defeat of two or three Ministers (Lands and
Railways particularly) is regarded as assured.
The S.A. Assembly of 54 members can be
“ sectionised ” thus: —Ministers, 6; Opposition-
ists, 5; Labour members, 10 ; Independents, 9 ;
Country Party, 23; and the Speaker. Thus the
Country Party and the direct Opposition, when
combined, just command an absolute majority,
and once or twice during the session, notably on
the Land Values and the Factories Bills, the union
took place.
Of all fallen idols, none has come to such
hopeless smash as Graham Berry, once high priest,
so far as lip-service went, of Victorian Radicalism.
The other day, at his old stronghold, Geelong, the
ennobbled liberal attended a municipal festivity,
and was seen, bedraggled, perspiring, and utterly
baked, hustling for a place on the outskirts of the
crowd. Later on he attempted to address the
assemblage, but was howled down and scoffed at,
and resumed his seat looking very white and
flabbergasted. Altogether, Berry is a dreadful
example to the Australian politician who yearns
to be Sir Something, and to wear a foreign gew-
gaw on his chest.

Parkes, if he stands for St. Leonards, will,
in all human probability, be beaten by E. M.
Clark, honest bawling auctioneer. So it is
rumoured that, making a virtue of something
which closely borders upon necessity, the old man
will hold back his nomination and contest with
Premier Dibbs any seat the latter may select,
with a view to utterly shutting him out of
Parliament.
Judge Hensman’s departure for England
and improbable return to W.A. is regarded as
meaning the appointment of Attorney-General
Burt to a judgeship, with George Leake as sue-
ceeding A.G. The ennobbled George Shenton
hopes to be made Attorney-General, and the be-
baubled Lee Steere to become President of the
Legislative Council, his Assembly tenure being
easily frangible.
Premier Dibbs’followerscharge Barton with
having “sold them like a bnllock at Smithfield. ,,
They sought to make him retract his resignation
and arranged a Cabinet meeting to discuss with
him the exact form his retractation should take,
but he failed to attend, walked straight into the
House, and “ hurst the show.” They suspect him
of coquetting with Parkes with a view to a
Ministry whose ostensible raison d'etre will be
Federation.
Attorney-General Burt (W.A.) : When
a candidate comes along, ask him if he will sup-
port the Forrest Ministry, If he says ‘ Yes,
put him in ;if he says ‘ No,’ put him out.” This
is like Septimus Burt, Q.C. He talks to juries in
a similar fashion. “ I think so and so; in fact, I
don’t see how it could have been_ otherwise; there-
fore, I ask you to give a verdict in accordance
with my opinion.”
Sam Griffith, much improved in health, is
understood to be disgusted with the obscurity in
which his acceptance of a Judgeship has landed
him. He pines for activity, and, had_ he his own
way, would resign and re-enter Parliament. It
is doubtful, though, whether ho could now politi-
cally hold his own against “Tommy” Byrnes, or,
in these depressed times, make anything like his
present income at the bar.
The hollow mockery of Robert Reid’s
“mission” to England and America was put
beyond question as soon as he had sailed from
Melbourne. One of Reid’s mightiest undertak-
ings was to be the fixing up of things at the Agent-
General’s office for a long interregnum period, a
task that could easily be accomplished by a short
official letter and a postage-stamp. Now comes
an announcement that a new Agent-General
(Duncan Gillies) is to be appointed immediately,
and people naturally desire to know the latest
excuse for the English portion of Reid’s expensive
trip.

Christmas more pleasant, and to assist in maintaining the good old
English custom of welcoming Santa Claus and making the outward and
visible signs of the season symbolical of the time of the year.” —Suburban
Newspaper Drivel.
Mr. De Dobson keeps up (while it’s 105 in the shade) a “good old
English” custom.

asked him what views he held upon the Sinking
Fund. “Hi refuse to hanswer,” was the response;
“ hand if it wasn’t for the hexalted position I
’old, hi would dab your ’ead hin your own paint-
pot.” Then there was so horrible a silence that
the audience could hear the interest accumulating
u P° n its overdraft,
Duncan Gillies is said, by his champion
journal, to have enjoyed the full confidence of the
present Victorian Assembly. He was its natural
leader, “ sitting, so to speak, above the Premier.”
But what an awful accusation to hurl at a man in
the form of a compliment! The natural leader of
that malodorous gang—the chief husher-up—pre-
dominant keepitdarker—principal dumb protector
of the bank-busting crowd !!! Gillies ought to
Argus for libel.
Cackling over the recent ocean - race
between the P. and O. s.s. Australia and the
French M.M. Co.’s Arraand Behic, a professedly
“ Australian” paper, a Sydney daily, says—“ The
sea is England’s, not so easily as of old, but still
undoubtedly.” Of course! The sea is England’s,
the earth is England’s, the sky is England’s, the
air is England’s, the equator is England’s, heaven
is England’s, hell is England’s—and, by Jingo, the
daily press is England’s,
The letters of thanks which retiring Crown
Ministers officially tender to their officers and
clerks are now generally laughed at by the
recipients. They are always tendered, they
always breathe the same sentiments, they always
extol the same virtues, and it very often happens
that the writers fall into using the same words
and phrases. Several lines in R. E. O’Connor’s
late letter to the N.S.W. Justice myrmidons were
identical with lines in the effusion which the
lamented Abigail bestowed upon “ my loyal co-
workers in this great Department of Mines.”
The vigour with which the Patterson
Ministry decides to hang Frances Knorr for baby-
murder is in strange contrast with the want of
vigour which characterised it when it “washed
its hands of the Mercantile prosecution,” and
refused to take any cognisance of James Munro’s
broken banks, and positively declined to move in
the smellful Freehold Investment case and many
other affairs of the same kind. The Ministry has
a reputation for energy to maintain, and, having
let so many influential suspects slip through its
boneless fingers, it is bound to hang the wretched
woman to show that it is still a “ strong Govern-
ment.”
Dear Bulletin, — Relative to the kingdom
of Greece’s proposed composition of 10s. in the £,
and your comments thereon—isn’t it a fact that Bull
has made more money out of some countries that
“let him in ” than from those which borrow at a
comparatively low rate of interest ? Turkey and
Egypt, for example, have been profitable clients
for John Isaac Moses Bull, despite their in-
solvencies. —Yours, Unearned Increment. [The
borrower that the money-lending Bull made most
out of was his own nation, which floated loans at
50 per cent, discount to raise money for the
French wars of George 111. and the Bottomless
Pitt; and the countries which let him in generally
did it before Bull had time to make much out of
them. Egypt, by the way, isn’t insolvent. —
Ed. B.]
Ed. Bulletin, —You say that “during the
worst of the depression the Vic. Government gave
out some =£2ooo worth of passes.” So it did, but
it is still doing it, and on the same conditions as
you mention. The other day a cab drove up to
the door of Melb. public offices and a well-dressed
young man with a radiant blue tie dashed from it
into the office. He wanted a pass to Warrackna-
beal—would the clerk please hurry up as he had
to catch a train. Asked what the cabman was
charging him, he replied carelessly: “Three bob
an hour.” He signed the agreement and had got
as far as the door when a happy thought struck
him. “By the way, I see this thing’s second-
class. If I pay the difference can I travel first?”
—Wat Tyler.
Dear Bulletin, —The Midland Railway in
Westralia is one of the most scandalous pieces of
political jobbery ever perpetrated. The Govern-
ment not only gave the co. thousands of acres of
good land to construct the line, but actually found
it £500,000 in hard cash. Indications are that
the province will have to pay that .£500,000, and
then buy back the line, before it can get rid of the
crowd mixed up in it. How much change then
out of .£1,400,000? Did you notice the State-rent
reductions of £250,000 to enable some members of
both Houses of Parliament and their friends to
re-stock their poverty-stricken runs, on which they
employ none but Chinese and aboriginals ? Well,
so soon as these people heard that a man from
another province was crossing the border into
W.A. with 10,000 head of cattle to stock an out-
side run they rushed the Stock Tax Bill (30s. per
head) through Parliament, and demanded from
this man, at the point of a police-trooper’s re-
volver, “£15,000, or take your cattle back.”
Until the “six families” no longer monopolise
this province, Westralia will remain a howling
dingoland. The best Xmas-box that W.A. could
receive would be the sudden death of some 50 or
60 of her so-called “ prominent citizens ” — alias
political jobbers—and their bummers. —Yours,
&c., Jarbah. Perth, W.A.
HOW THEY SAVED THE COUNTRY.
“ From every imaginable quarter Peers were whipped-
up to give their vote against the Home Rule Bill and
make its defeat as impressive as possible.”— (Daily
Paper.)
The Duke he came from his kennels down ;
The Earl was carted from Bedlam town ;
The Baron deserted his Hebrews true
(And left unpaid some bills past due);
The Bishop came in his shirt of hair
From his lowly hut with its walls so bare ;
The Marquis came on his keeper’s chain
With an ice-pack cooling his heated brain ;
Lord Bevis came from the boozing ken;
Lord Guy he came from the harlot’s den—
For the maddening beat of the Tory drum
It rolled and rang in a fury : “ Come 1
O ! come, and save the country 1 ”
O ! Some were sober and some were not,
And some were short of a tile (I wot);
With a martial flash in the fishy eye
And a doddering gait they hobbled by;
They hastened downward for their lives,
They left their homes and their neighbours’ wives;
They even left (these patriots true)
The ballet-girl and the billet-doux;
They left their royalties—left their rent,
And helter-skelter away they went,
At the maddening beat of the Tory drum
As it rolled and rang in a fury ; “ Coma !
O ! come and save the country ! ”
Q’sland.
P. LUETIG.
The Political Outlook.
The most appropriate and significant descrip-
tion of political affairs in New South Wales, at
the present moment, is “in chaos.” The light of
representation is absolutely extinguished. Is it not
time for electors of this country to be up and doing,
and say, like theßomans in the days gone by, “We
will suffer wrongs no more?” Let every elector re-
flect, and grasp the situation, then, when the time
has run and the day is at hand when the battle
shall be fought, there will be—there must be—such
an overwhelming opinion given as to the base
treachery and subtle scheming of the past few
days, that on all sides the acknowledgment will
be heard, “ The voice of the people is the voice of
God.” Such an acknowledgment as that has for
years been heralded in regard to the original
panacea for debility, lassitude, and weakness,
“ Marshall’s Tonic Nervine,” and for the coming
struggle, when staunch nerves, indomitable pluck,
and fearless resolution will be absolute necessities,
let everyone fortify himself with this super-
lative, recuperative, and vitalising agent. Mr.
Whiteford, late of the Herald office, further
testifies to the above by writing. “My wife,
after taking two or three bottles of Marshall’s
Tonic Nervine, rapidly improved in appetite,
digestion, and consequently nerve vigour. She
was before rapidly sinking, now she is strong and
well. ”

Gold discoveries. Very rich fields have
been opened at Southern Cross, Coolgardie, Mur-
chison, &c., in West Australia, which will bo one
of the greatest gold-producing countries of the
world. Land in Perth and Albany, the principal
cities, is ridiculously low in price, and must cer-
tainly increase very much in value in the next
few years. One of the best investments at present
time is to secure a few lots. The Intercolonial
Investment Co., Ltd., 309 Pitt-street, Sydney,
offers exceptionally well-positioned blocks from
,£lO each on very easy terms, or for cash,
guaranteed title. The company has been estab-
lished in Sydney and Perth for many years, and
is well-known throughout the colonies for straight
dealing. The manager is Mr. W. J, Douglas,
who will be pleased to forward plans to any
address, and, having a personal knowledge of the
properties, will select faithfully for intending
purchasers where required.

No representative Australian cricketer
must again be allowed to take his wife abroad on
a team-tour. The captain of a mail-steamer is not
permitted to bring his wife—on the principle that
there must be only one captain on board a ship.
The principle obviously applies to cricket-teams.
# “ * #
The wife of an Australian Judge having
gone ’Ome, her learned husband began to philander
with a shapely singing- woman. Hearing that
Madame was about to return, his Honor visited
his melodious friend and explained that his posi-
tion as a Judge would not permit of the all too
delightful intimacy being continued, but he would
take the liberty of calling again and offering her
some little memento of their—er —friendship.
Later on he did call bearing a heavy parcel, which,
when undone, proved to contain—a family Bible.
You should just hear his Honor’s learned brother,
the chief of the divorce-court, relate this little
story.
» « •
Mr. Giles, S.M., of Sydney, on Boxing
Day disposed of 147 cases, mostly “drunks,” in
40 min. Never since the world’s foundation were
there 147 cases heard on one day which did not
.each on the average call for more than 20
seconds’ attention on the part of the judge. Mr.
Giles is on such beastly familiar terms with the
Goddess of Justice that he publicly chucks her
under the chin.

AT GEEY GREEN DAWN.
(For The Bulletin.)
A deep sweet sleep I fell in,
And I dreamt a dream of joy,
/ nd in waking I murmured, “ Helen I”
1 had dreamt of Helen of Troy.
The woman I love was bending
Above me with lightning gleams
In her eyes : “Though your love’s unending,”
She said, “I dislike your dreams.”
“ And what if the dreams were true then ? ”
I whispered, nestling my head
On her lily breast —“ As for you then
I would strangle you, dear,” she said.
V. J. DALEY.
Australia spent a very jerry Xmas.
A man got a divorce
recently, in Melbourne, on
the ground that his wife had
deserted him “about thirty
times altogether.” Their
married life extended over
a period of fifteen years, so
it would seem that the lady
left him every half-year on
an average. This exercise
probably did a lot to keep
her in good health. The
sedentary life led by most
women is very injurious.
* * #
Auckland (M.L.) Wes-
leyans are much perturbed
with regard to the proper
method of running their
paper, the Methodist. One
rev. gentleman said that they
would “ never get a paper
to please them unless it was
published in Heaven, and
edited by Gabriel.” That is
a good suggestion, but maybe
Gabriel isn’t a Wesleyan.
* * #
In Auckland (M.L.) a
man lately got two years for
stealing an opera-glass. Then
another man was brought up,
charged with a brutal and
horrible assault on a little
girl, and the Judge gave him
two years also.

“See, amid the winter
snow, born for us on earth
below,” runs the presumptu-
ously ignorant h’old Hinglish
carol. Christ was born in a
warm country, anyway,
apart from the question as to
the time of year when the
event took place. But the
typical Christmas of Great
Britain is snow-bound, and
no other Christmas is sup-
posed to have a voice in
the matter. Most people
are aware that Hingland
bought the sole rights in the
Saviour’s birth.
Another love-story, from N.S.W, divorce-
court. After the unyoking the divorcing wife
said on several occasions that she was still
attached to her husband, and was willing to for-
give him and live with him again. The solicitor
caused a communication to be sent to respondent
on the subject, but he said he preferred to remain
in gaol.
# • •
On the morning of Dec. 28th, 250,000
(more or less) married men read aloud to their
wives the news that seven senseless politicians
had conspired to invite the Duke of York and
bride to visit Australia; 250,000 left male
eyelids winked when the announcement was
completed, and the same number of ladies said,
“The idea! as though she hadn’t something else
to think about.” There wasn’t a woman in the
land who couldn’t see further than a Premier.
Unless Premier Patterson, and all the other
Australian Premiers, had gathered from private
information that the Duchess of York might have
reasons for wishing to take a long sea voyage,
instead of an overwhelming reason for not wishing
to do so, then their joint-and-several invitation
scheme was childishly foolish on the face of it.
Haven’t they wives to give them a judicious hint?
Apart from domestic contingencies, the Yorks
would find a few score of objections to rushing off
to Australia just now. The Duke’s aged grand-
mother and nervous mother would object. The
Duchess’s parents would sooner fall out of the
window than willingly let the prospective queen
of their family visit the other end of the earth
until the “direct succession is further provided
for.” Finally, the Yorks themselves, having any
amount of fun cut and dried for consumption
whole battalions of relatives, friends and toadies
waiting to entertain them—would, one imagines,
regard the Pattersonian idea as a crazy joke. And
it was indeed a very crazy joke. Nevertheless, its
lunacy appealed to the mind of the Australian
daily press, and won plaudits from every import-
ant editor whose imagination requires greasing.
Didn’t The Bulletin mention (in rhyme) a few
weeks ago that the Queen was anxiously looking
forward to “ the first of May ? ” Wasn’t that good
enough for the seven statesmanlike dodderers who
thought the present time “ most opportune ” for
a visit to Australia? The Bulletin is infallible
with its Royal Family information. The Duke of
York was apportioned to Princess May by this
red-covered journal long before the matter was
settled anywhere else.

ONE LAST APPEAL.
“At this juncture, too, a \isit from the Duke of
York and his young wife would do much good, in
raising the spirits of these communities after the late
depression which we have all felt so much.”— {Extract
from Premier Patterson’s immortal circular note.)
Come over, Dook, and do your best
Your level best,
To cheer a people sore depress’t—
Our state of mind is fearful!
Come here and be a buttered guest,
And set the troubled souls at rest
Of thousands who are tearful.
For since pur banks began to break—
Began to break,
We’ve suffered from the pocket-ache ;
Beloved Dook, for Hingland’s sake 1
Consent to make us cheerful.
When stocks and shares are going down—
Hi, derry-down!
The jokes of pantaloon and clown
Seem anything but novel.
BALLAST FOR THE HUT.
SQUATTER: “Now then, you are not going to camp in that hut.
You’d best crawl off the run!”
MOULDY MIKE: “If it was’nt for the trav'lers sleepin’ in it
your bloomin’ hut would crawl off itself.”
There’s nothing doing round the town ;
Our only comfort is the Crown
That lets us file a shovel.
With muffled sigh and meek “ hooray ” —•
Alack a day !
We bless yourself and Princess May,
And on our knees we humbly pray—
“ Assist us, Lord, to grovel.”
Come over, Dook, and bring your bride—
Your royal bride,
We need a “ burst ” of joy and pride,
A “drunk” of adoration ;
At banquets you’ll be glorified
(What loyal lies would then be lied !)
By snobs of rank and station.
Triumphal arches we’ll erect,
So gaily deck’t;
A “Splendid Welcome ” please f xpect;
Again we cry, with deep respect,
Come out and save the nation !
’Twas Patterson who struck the plan
The artful plan;
He is a tough old Orangeman—
You never met a tougher.
He smileth on the Munro clan
And hates the rebel Fenian
Or democratic “duffer.”
In fancy he is placing now—
Is placing now
A coronet upon his brow,
And jubilantly thinking how
The Radicals would suffer.

Come over, Dook and Duchess fair,
Exalted pair,
Twin spectacles sublime and rare
For legislative jobber;
Disperse the clouds of dark despair,
And make each heart that harbours care
A light, ecstatic throbber.
Fill all the land with song and dance —
The merry dance;
Bid biped asses neigh and prance—
Give Australasia a chance —
A decent chance to slobber.

# # #
A Bulletin man the other day attended
the funeral of a Catholic gentleman (in Queens-
land, of course—nearly everything happens in
Queensland). A particularly stale cleric was in
attendance. He was a short, squat ecclesiastic
with a red face covered with the shine of old,
neglected perspiration. His hair hadn’t been
brushed recently, and a great mass of red and not
over-clean fat bulged out over his dirty, tight
collar, giving him the appearance of a man who
was being strangled while hot. He had no neck
worth speaking of, and his large bullet head, with
protruding veins just below the grease-mark of
his hat and just above his inflamed nose, gave him
a most aggressive aspect. Two dirty, hairy,
freckled paws with short fingers like sausages and
a deep rich loam beneath the nails, formed the
balance of him so far as he was visible. The out-
ward sign of his holy calling was a cassock that
was ripped at the seams for want of a tidy house-
keeper to repair it, and shiny at the back; and he
looked as if he had been dressed with a fork.
There were grease-spots on the good man, and he
was vaguely suggestive of snuff, onions and anger.
This over-ripe individual hurried through the
service in a thick voice, so unctuous as to suggest
that his utterances were sifted through ham.
Then, when he had mumbled the necessary Latin,
he felt in his waistcoat pocket for the holy water
with which to sprinkle the deceased. The waist-
coat was old, stale, grease-spotted, and redolent
of pipe-ash like the rest of him, and the holy water
was in an essence-of-lemon bottle with the label
still on. He brought it out and made a hurried
grab at the cork, but it broke off short, and the
holy father grew purple with sudden indignation.
Then he cocked his eye sideways at the remains of
the cork, and hurriedly fishing out a knife with a
broken blade he opened it with his murky nails
and proceeded to gouge anxiously—there were a lot
of people present—at the obstacle. Meanwhile the
proceedings were at a standstill. The cork, despite
all the good man’s gouging, wouldn’t move, and
with every effort he grew redder and madder, and
his black and blue muzzle, where he was in urgent
want of a shave, began to champ ominously. At
last the knife broke. Then a sudden thought
striking him he poked the cork in with his black
little finger, and hastily sprinkled the coffin.
Soon afterwards the impressive ceremony ended.
It was a mean, squalid affair from first to last,
and to the clean Catholics present, who, no doubt,
hope to be buried decently by clean priests, it was
obviously most unspeakably disgusting.

More Argus: “The Princess May is
known to everyone, not only because of the
troubles that she has undergone, but” etc., etc.
This is nice for Prince George. It’s always the
man she didn’t get about whom a woman feels
“bad,” but one hardly expects a journal like the
Argus, so eminently respectable and full of “good
taste,” to persistently picture “our future Queen”
in a wilted attitude at the sarcophagus of a former
mash.
• • ■
Jules Joubert is again to the fore, pro-
moting a “Tasmanian Exhibition” at Hobart—
perhaps the very last promotable possibility in
exhibitions. The Tasmanian Exhibition is 12
months distant, as yet; but all Hobart is already
steadily choir-practising for its inaugural shriek-
music. It is a common evening occurrence to
meet a procession of sets of choristers—four
women to one small man is the usual set—with
music under their arms, and amours in their hearts.
The amount of social responsibility now being
undertaken by the imperturbable Joubert cannot
be estimated by any known process of calculation,
but when the final results of the choir-practices
are consummated, the total should be interesting
to sociologists. M. Joubert strikes many persons
as being a dilution of De Lesseps—this is intended
as a compliment—just as Surgeon Fitzgerald, of
Melbourne, and perhaps also the. much travelled
R. S. Smythe, appear to be dilutions of the Great
Napoleon.

And at Newcastle, Brisbane and Toowoomba.
good little boy called Henry in Mrs. Wood’s
novels—the boy who dies young of consumption.
The lawyer employed him (at first to clean boots)
and educated him, and when, later on, he had
become a law-clerk with a large hole in his
lung the kindly lawyer gave him £5 and sent him
out on to a friend’s station in the Far West.
There he took part in making the biggest silver
find on earth, and by-and-bye he came back to
town wearing a collar as high as a sheep-hurdle
and a fan-tailed diagonal coat, and looked up the
lawyer and told him that he Was worth .£BOO,OOO,
after which he went “home” and discovered his
relations, who were, of course, people of noble
birth. /
The story serves to show how, provided he has
phenomenal luck, the respectful small boy, who
cleans the boots assiduously, may rise in the world
and come to 'great affluence. Still there are a
few conspicuous omissions in it. When Philip
returned the boom was over, and the Melbourne
lawyer had doubtless been ravaged by it like all
other Melbourne lawyers, and the ashes of his
burnt hopes were conspicuous in his silvery hair.
Yet Philip isn’t stated to have wept on the neck
of his good, benevolent old master and asked him
whether a cheque would be of any service. This
omission (possibly due to Philip’s modesty) is
very poor encouragement to the next lawyer to
take in and educate the next small boy who wants
boot-cleaning employment. Also, the discovery
of his elevated relatives at “ ’Ome,” and the
triumphal return to the moated grange, or the
hereditary towers, or whatever it was, is a
depressing penny - dreadful incident; and the
moral of it all is that if Philip hadn’t got a hole
in his lung he would probably have remained a
lawyer’s drudge to this day. Therefore, the pros-
pects of the small boy who isn’t sufficiently
diseased don’t seem to amount to much.
* » *
In some of the big Anglo-Australian liners
the under-stewards and stewardesses get £1 to
£l.los. a month, depending entirely on saloon
tips to make £5 a month. This, however, they
fail to do on the trip out, even with a full passenger
list—unless there should be a fair percentage of
returning Australians aboard. The “Home”
trip, with a full saloon, usually results in a
“fiver” for the man who is sufficiently adroit
with his “gentlemen.” The stewardesses prosper
according to the number of fat lady-passengers.
Thin women crush for a poor dividend, says an
expert.

At a Maryborough (Q.) hotel. Waitress :
“Mr. Matheson wants a room.” Proprietor:
“Give him the room wid the marble wash-stand
on the front balcony.” Waitress : “Oh, it’s not
the Railway Commissioner, but Linsey, Woolsey
and Co.’s traveller.” Proprietor: “Then give
him the small room near the bath.”

The morgue has many secrets. When a
drowned lady comes to be undressed there the
true history of a water-party is sometimes more
clearly revealed than by the statements of the
survivors as related in the newspapers.

According to a recent Tasmanian decision,
it is not illegal for a woman to wear male attire,
but it is criminal for a man to don a woman’s
clothes. The position seems logical, somehow.
Trousered women aren’t things of beauty, but men
in petticoats always strike The Bulletin as
deserving six months,

Tasmania has been somewhere described
as a place the chief products of which are those
which turned man out of Paradise—snakes, apples

Dear Bulletin, — lie the leprosy in Aus-
stralia. In the Northern Territory of S.A. a
strong feeling exists among the whites that the
disease is firmly implanted in the aborigines,
Years ago they had one black on the leper-station
an undoubted case. Subsequently someone
wrote the local paper from Macarthur River,
pointing out what he declared to be leprosy in a
wholesale form in the blacks’camp of that district.
Later still, it was reported that native lepers were
to be found at the Alligator River. Ten years
age- the Chinese prophesied that the whites would
eventually catch it. It would go, said the Mon-
from 1
S.A* seems to care a rap about the black leper
but there will be a sensation when the yellow
pagan’s prophecy is fulfilled, and some influential
citizens are deposited on the leper island for the
remainder of their decaying days.~M.

Lne strong matrimonial tendencies of
middle-aged maidenhood are apparent in the
romance of Catherine Hart, a Melbourne cook,
Catherine, who has arrived at years of discretion
mall matters, save nuptial sentiment, was resting
on a seat in the Fitzroy Gardens when a strange
man came to woo. After a few more tender meet-
mgs, Catherine brought her friend up to the
scratch by telling him that she intended to sail
for England, with £250 of capital, less the passage
(“ single ” passage) money. “ Let us get married
and sail together ?’’ proposed the base deceiver.
bonnie Kate fell against his beating
heart,, find gave him £lBO to buy tickets for two.
The Melbourne police “ think there is little doubt
that the man has gone away ” withoqt waiting for
the ceremony, and a deserted spinster
realises that the road to conjugal bliss is perfor-
ated with pitfalls, especially for the party who is
unduly eager to get there. This is about the
saddest short love-story ever printed by The
Bulletin.
* * m
Volunteer-Captain Honey (brother of the
champion N.S.W. rifle-shot), drowned with his
wife in the Kiama boating disaster, whereby seven
people in all perished, was a brave and generous
man, the record of whose self-sacrifice by no
means depends upon embellished statements of
the sort ordinarily current after sensational
accidents. He was a fine swimmer and died in a
gallant effort to save his wife. With his people,
as with Mrs. Wood, whose husband and three
children died at the same time, there is felt the
utmost sympathy.

Recently, at an Adelaide Executive meet-
ing, the flunkey announced “ His Excellency, the
Lieutenant Governor! ” and in marched that
bachelor functionary’s famous Pomeranian pup,
with its tail erect. Mr. Way was quite half a
minute in the rear. It is expected that the pup
will soon be gazetted an extra aide-de-camp.
Veneration, —Respect for the ignorant
past.
The fact that Robert Stout, the boss
atheist of Maoriland, got a larger share of the
female vote, and was canvassed for more vigorously
by the female propagandists than any other can-
didate at the recent elections, seems to break up
some old-established theories about the orthodoxy
of women. Or else it may be that women are
teetotal first and orthodox afterwards, and when
the choice lies between the cold tea infidel and the
drinky Christian they prefer the former. Any-
how, the egg-bald Stout didn’t get in on his good
looks.
Though each man had necessarily to con-
tribute no more than =£6o, the money pooled by
the first contingent of “New Australians”
averaged >£loo per head, or more than £20,000 in
all. The common fund is said to have benefited
still more largely through the capital voluntarily
surrendered by the second batch of emigrants.
There are over 3000 names on the Australian roll,
and nearly 1000 on the Maoriland registers. Para-
lanea starts on a fair cash basis, anyhow, and can-
not refer any future eventualities to want of
foundationary means.
The once dignified Argus heaps common
cuss-language on the Age :
The Melbourne morning journal whose disregard for
strict veracity is notorious cannot apparently help study-
ing our articles, though they have unfortunate conse-
quences of provoking severe attacks of foaming at the
mouth. The journal in question comes hack again and
again to the study of the articles. And the more often
they are read the more painful the exhibition of the
constitutional weakness. Now, whatever else may be
said of Ananias, he kept his temper. Our proposal cannot
be challenged on its merits, but it can be lied about, and
it is lied about. Lies are told as to its origin ; lies are
told as to its meaning ; lies are told as to our past utter-
ances ; lies are scattered broadcast in the form of dis-
honest quotations. Now, Ananias seems only to have
told one lie.
There was a time when the Argus regarded the
Age with the lofty contempt of an archangel
regarding a dead worm. And now
Says a correspondent.:—
In 1846 the Stockton cloth factory (at Newcastle,
N.S. W.) throve so that it. was able to turn out billiard-
cloths cheaper than the imported article. Then, the
manufacture of these cloths was considered as one of the
highest achievements of the art, and was usually entrusted
only to experts. The high freights ruling between this
country and Europe constituted for many years an ample
protection for the little industry', which then employed
over 80 hands, but, with the introduction of steam and
cheap freights, it languished and died. So effectually
was it knocked out that in ’5B the whole building was in
ruins, and a few years later had wholly disappeared. No
trace of it now remains—in fact, not a tenth of the pre-
sent inhabitants of Stockton know that there was ever
such an institution in the district.
When the Orange Free State (S. Africa)
was on the eve of defeating the neighbouring
tribe of Basutos, Britain, fearing the loss of
eligible territory, arose at Heaven’s command,
and so successfully negotiated for mercy for the
poor nigs, that the simple Basutos looked upon
Britain as their friend, and petitioned for a
Government Resident. They got him, and
missionaries, too; and lo ! behold another slice of
territory grabbed by J. Bull. That was a score or
so years back, and to-day that same holy Britain
which exhorted the Orange Free State to treat
the nigs, with mercy—snuffle—is shooting down
the Matabele with machine-guns. Some ten years
after the British Resident’s advent to Basutoland,
Cape Parliament passed a Bill enacting that all
natives under the “Crown ” were to disarm. The
Basutos objecting, Gen. Gordon, then Com-
mandant-General of the Cape forces, was des-
patched to put down the “ revolt.” As he entered
Basutoland the native school-children met him
singing, and Gordon, overcome thereat, wheeled
round Ins horse’s head, and returned to the Cape
to resign his Commission. “You send me to
stamp out a revolt,” he said, “ and they meet me
singing hymns !” There always were more of the
ingredients of the successful missionary than of
tile soldier about Gordon.
Gold,—Tho visible sign of man's universal
distrust.
From Victor Cohen’s report to the Vic-
torian Cricket Council: “The team had the dis-
tinguished honour of lunching with H.R.H. the
Duke of Connaught during the progress of the
match at Portsmouth. To his Grace the Earl of
Sheffield thanks are especially due.” In his
reverence for the salt of the earth Cohen gives the
Earl’s title a ducal style. Which again reminds
us of the British bookmaker who yelled out to the
Duke of Hamilton in the betting-ring at New-
market —“Won’t you take my ten monkeys to
one, yer Royal Tghness?” “He isn’t a Tgh-
ness,” whispered a friend who had managed to
pick up the code of nobility. “Ain’t he—why I
thought the was a dook ! ” replied the bookie.
Lord Gormanston, Governor of Tasmania,
a Hibernian colossus, lately bad introduced to
him, at choir-practice in connection with the
forthcoming Hobart Exhibition, a very little
fiddler with long, rat-sucked-looking hair, and that
far-away expression known among young ladies
as spirituelle. The Jumboesque Governor, tower-
ing above the catgut virtuoso, proceeded to
descant on exhibitions he had seen, and particu-
larly on the “wan of eighteen-fifty-wan,” which,
he remarked, was perhaps the best though not the
biggest of the lot, “After all,” remarked the
Gormanston, drawing himself up to his full
75 or 80 inches, “ soize is no critayrian.” Nobody
laughed—in Hobart Viceroyalty is sacred, and
theatre dress-circle audiences line the passage to
let the Governor get out first—but the little
fiddler looked immensely relieved.
“Barney Parkes” is what a Melbourne
paper calls old man Parkes’s son. The aforesaid
“ Barney ” is getting on for his half-century now,
yet he is still only young Parkes, old Parkes’s son.
The ghastly and horrible youthfulness of old
Parkes makes it impossible to regard young
Parkes as anything but a jam-smeared infant, and
no one takes him for a serious, middle-aged
architect. Then, when old Parkes dies, young
Parkes will come out of his shadow and become
hoary in a day. It is his awful fate to have no man-
hood at all, but to jump from infancy to old age
in one fatal act. If old Parkes kept on living,
young Parkes would continue to be young Parkes
at 94. A man with an overshadowing parent like
that must emigrate if he wants to have an in-
dividuality of his own. By-the-way, there is a
great opening for a sanatorium in some remote
and healthy spot for elderly young men with
oppressive fathers.
Carter Harrison, the recently-shot Mayor
of Chicago, is alleged to have delivered the fol-
lowing address to a party of French engineers
who visited the great Pork Fair :
You have shown us the way, but I reckon we shall very
soon beat you. lam not an engineer, but am the Mayor,
and as such I direct the engineers. lam also head of the
Fire Brigade, and I extinguish fires. By virtue of this
star you see on my breast, I command all the policemen
in this city, I could order them to put you all in prison,
as I could order them to release you if I chose. You will
say lam a great person. I think so, too. Xam the first
man in Chicago. Chicago is the first city in Illinois.
Illinois is the first State of the United States, and the
United States is the greatest country in the world. I am,
therefore, the greatest man in the world. I invite you
and your children to come to the next World’s Fair at
Chicago. By that time, Chicago will be the greatest and
most beautiful city of the world, and beside it London
will be a village and Paris will be nothing at all.
“ Chicago is a hog city,” said somebody or other,
“and the people who live there are hogs.”
A Magnificent Adjustment.
The watch, undoubtedly, is a fine piece of mechan-
ism. It has lately been brought to a very
high state of perfection. One of the most wonder-
ful pieces in the whole of the escapement is the
fine spiral spring commonly known as the hair
spring. The way in which this coil is tempered
is truly marvellous. For many years it was a per-
fect secret, and even now certain factories pride
themselves upon the perfection and exceptionally
fine temper they throw into the spring. Let either
the main or hair spring break and the whole train
comes to a standstill. Both are indispensable, and
each must have perfect action. Now what these
springs are to the watch, the liver and kidneys are
to the body. On the one hand you have the liver
in the form of a large gland or secreting organ
separating bile from the blood, and also making
glycogen from it; while on the other hand you
have the kidneys in the shape of two glands, and
consisting of many tubes, removing the water and
urea from the blood, and forming a part of the ex-
cretory system. As long as these two act in unison
a general healthy order prevails. Let the kidneys,
however, become granulated and Bright’s disease,
with its train of suffering and anguish, makes its
appearance. The uric acid is not carried off, and
the man becomes the subject of distressing afflic-
tions. There is, however, a specific for all liver
and kidney complaints, and its cures are simply
wonderful. Warner’s “ Safe ” Cure has pro ved
itself a boon to thousands and a treasure worth
more than its weight in gold. By keeping these
two springs of action in their normal condition it
secures an almost perfect immunity from danger
to the whole of the body.

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHA.T IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpenoe, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at

The Wild Cat Column.
President David Storey (N.S.W. Commercial
Travellers’ Association) has called upon drummers gener-
ally to prop up distrusted banks and “financial institutions
which are the great mainstay and a great assistance to
commercial men ay, to prop up these potentialities by
word o f mouth when the garrulous bagman is on his
travels. Gorgeous idea 1 Solid foundation—the cast-iron
Jaw of the collective drummer, the jaw that never told
a cram. Stupendous scheme I “ Confidence ”is hereby
restored.
* * ♦
Judging by the recent quotations for bank
shares, the blessed “restoration of confidence” is still
afar off, like the returning prodigal when he first came
under the old man’s notice. - A. J.S. Bank shares (£5 153-
paid up) are offering in an unresponsive market at £2 ss.
The City of Sydney’s £8 shares are offering at £4 ss. and
its £6 shares at £3. The 70s. shares of the Q.N.B. can’t
find buyers at 15s. National of Australasia (90s. paid up>
are going at 31s. 6d. The Bank of Victoria’s 555. shares
are offered unsuccessfully at the awful figure of 65.; and
the B.N.Z.’s shares (£5 ss. paid) can be had for £3 16s.
Of all the lot the shares of the B.N.Z. are the only ones
which offer any specially attractive prospect. When
that institution reconstructed (without closing) some
1 ears ago, a large number of unrealisable properties were
transferred to an Estates Co. at a low valuation which
was in keeping with the blankness of the times and the
hopeles ness of the general outlook. The Bank of
N.Z. holds most of the shares in this co., and with the
general revival in Maoriland they promise, in course of
time, to bring back almost all the money that was lost by
the bank in the bad time.
* • •
The alleged sales of Melb. properties these times
are a mirthless farce. Every now and then some dis-
tracted boom-bank resolves on a special effort to get rid
of a few dozen or a few hundred suburban allotments.
Accordingly, an expensive catalogue is printed, a lot of
advertising is done, the reserve-prices are fixed at a
minimum which makes the ghosts of the old boomers
weep in their graves, and the sale comes off. But nothing
is ever sold. Nobody dreams of offering anything even
distantly resembling the reserve. A small crowd gathers
and looks at the auctioneer with a despondent eye, and
that is all. The other day, by way of a bold experiment,
the Freehold Investment Co. decided to auction a few
properties with no reserve at all, but one such experiment
is likely to be enough. Land that fetched £lO a foot
three years ago went for 205., and ground valued approxi-
mately at £lOO an acre was sold with great difficulty at
something like £9. The theory that vacant land in the
Melb. suburbs has fallen 50 per cent, was knocked on the
head there and then ; it has fallen 90 per cent., and even
the remaining 10 per cent, looks shaky and uncertain.
• • •
The Bank of Van Diemen’s Land lottery is ab
last fairly under way. The ruins of the old bank are to
be disposed of in £300,000 shares of £1 each. There are
over 370 prizes, the first one being a Hobart city property
valued at £26,000. The second prize is a first-class city
hotel (Hadley’s “Orient”) of between 60 and 70 apart-
ments, value £22,500. Then follow one £12,000 prize;
one of £10,500 ; one of £9500 ; two valued at £BOOO each,
and so on down to a humble minimum of £lOO. The
prizes are all put in at a reasonable valuation, and the
lottery is managed (on behalf of the liquidators of the
V.D.L. Bank)by Geo. Adams, the well-known “Tattersall,”
whose name is sufficient guarantee for the solid integrity
of the whole proceedings. The properties are all situated
in Tasmania—a pleasant place where any man can put in
his declining years with satisfaction, and the winner of
any one of the 16 or 20 big prizes can’t do better than
settle down on his property, and let the restful, sleepy
atmosphere of Van Diemen’s Land soak into his soul, and
sink into a state of unutterable rest.
The V.D.L. shareholders, by the way, have lost
til their capital, and the success of the lottery, which is a
fair gamble duly authorised by Parliament, will simply
mean that they will be able to pay their debts without
making a call.
• * •
Almost the only Xmas presents which circulated
in Melbourne this year were official notifications setting
forth that the receiver had been put on the “ B ” list of
contributories In a broken bank the “A ” list of con-
tributories consist of the actual shareholders at the time
of the suspension. This lot seldom pan out for any-
thing worth speaking of; even 6d. in the £is usually
unattainable. Then the liquidators proceed to squeeze
the “B” list, which consists of people who sold their
shares to individuals on the “A” list, and who mostly
imagined that their liability had long since vanished.
But the Australian boom-bank bursts both the man who
stays in and the one who gets out. The latter individual
loses all claim on the profits, if there are any, but he
retains a vested interest in the losses, and is liable to be
sold up just the same. The man who buys a bank share
buys an incubus that, in most cases, he never altogether
gets rid of. He is like the person who carries about with
him the secret of a mangled corpse hidden in a cave, or
the knowledge of an unburied crime which all the
monkey soap on earth won’t wash off his soul. Last
year most of the broken banks of Melbourne about
exhausted the “ A ” list of contributaries, and the process
of skinning “ B ” list is just commencing in real earnest.

As a consequence of the style in which Sydney
banks are treating small manufacturers and other
“ little ” employers of labour, before the first quarter of
’94 expires a large number of these will be themselves
