Melbourne is not to be outdone by Sydney. A
4ft 6in seam of good brown coal has just been found by
drill at Red Bluff, near St. Hilda, and it is believed that
black coal lies beneath the brown.
» * •
The latest news the writer has from Coolgardie
is that both gold and water are increasing in Bayley’s
Reward ; that work on several of the outside claims is
proving the existence of big lodes of stone, poor in com-
parison with Bayley’s specimen crushings, but good
enough for several ounces to the ton, and that the lasting
character of the field is rapidly being established,
• * *
News from Coolgardie is spasmodic, indefinite,
and unreliable. A wire from Southern Cross to the field
would cure this. We hear of many thousands of ozs. of
gold, of other rich finds, but get precious little detail.
What the public wants to know : size and value of ore-
body, quantity in sight, nature of formations as well as
country rock, character of gold, and, above all, the per-
manent or short-lived features of reefs—is seldom pub-
lished.
» # #
No two plans of Coolgardie (as shown on the
prospectuses of newly-floated companies) seem to agree.
Each has its lease placed next door to the Reward, which
must have a tremendous circumference if all the claims
said to adjoin it really do so. Another perplexing thing
is the direction of the Reward reef, which appears to
wander terribly. In one plan it runs north and south (if
the projected co.’s lease occupies one of those positions),
whilst in another it runs east and west. And then how
do the promoters scoop ! In one 00. recently floated, the
capital was £lO,OOO, of which £7OOO went to the pro-
moters ; and at the first meeting it was calmly announced
that the co.’s first work would be to prospect for gold 1
They didn’t even know if they had a reef.
# • #
The Bayley Reward agony is piled up by the
exhibition in Perth of the latest cake, 68800 z., value,
£25,000. What will the crushings he when they come 7
It is understood that practically the whole of the gold
sold so far comes from a rich thin thread in the reef—the
value of the balance whereof is, so far, publicly uncertain ;
it may yield lOOoz. per ton, or, on the other hand, it may
only give lOdwts. Suppose the controllers of the claim
find the main body of reef poor, will they include any of
the golden thread in their crushings, so as to make up a
leoent average, or will they let the bulk stone take its
chance? Those without definite information on this
point- which the owners are in nowise bound to give—
had better not plunge in Bayley’s shares.
mm*
When the rain comes it will be—“ Go West,
young man, go West.” There are thousands going to
Coolgardie “in the wet season.” Then blessed are they
who have remained there in the dry.
• • •
Spa shares have risen from 6d. to Is. 3d. in a
week ; but everything on the Shoalhaven is sick from a
market view. Maorilanders who have seen the sluicing
arrangements there declare them amateurish and a long
way behind the age. If two-thirds of the gold has not been
lost, then the prospects taken from thousands of places
altogether have deceived many mining men.
• • *
Some shareholders are furious at the hole-and-
corner policy pursued by the directors of the Spa Sluicing
Co. The mine is nearly £4OOO in debt, and the only hope
appears to be the new manager’s confidence that by quick-
silver they will be able to save the finest gold.
* • *
The manager of Shoalhaven Consolidated Sluic-
ing Co. doesn’t think he has lost any gold. If not, then
it is the poorest wash on earth; almost as much gold
could be got out of Sydney street-sweepings. A return
from 1000 cubic yards of wash from a new face averaged
over fid. to the yard, which sounds well.
* * #
Active work has started at the rich Burraga
copper mine, N.S.W., owned by one man (Lewis Lloyd),
who works it only when it will pay.
• • •

proved a pocket. He must have thought the Reward
claim another, or he would never have sold five-sixths of

Mount Morgan Co. applies for six months’
labour exemption for nearly all the land outside the
freehold, on the ground that it is unprofitable. 180 to
200 men will lose work.
# * «
A big body of mundic ore, assaying over 300 z.,
has been found outside Mt. Morgan mine on the southern
boundary.
* * *
They have been trying cyanide at Croydon
during the past few months. Results so far satisfactory.
One mill has just completed erection of a plant with
capacity of 200 tons monthly. The Croydon Quartz
Crushing _Co. likely to follow suit with larger works to
treat their 60,000 tons of tailings. Altogether over
150,000 tons ready for operations on the field.

The only deep Block claim working on Croydon
is the Iguana Extended, in which a contract has just been
let to crosscut for the Iguana reef, at a vertical depth of
260 ft.

Newman’s syndicate, formed to search for gold
on Kerr’s estate, in the vicinity of the Wentworth mines,
N.S.W., has collapsed. Some Sydney men who had the
promise of this privilege stood aside for Newman’s crowd.
Will they now step in ?

Irwin, formerly manager of the Wentworth
mines, appears to he slowly, but surely, pulling the old
Brown’s Creek mine around. With big money, which it
may get, and capable management, which it has, it may
yet become a big mine.

* • *
Another creep on B.H. Proprietary, this time
some distance from the famous Block 11 disturbances, in
a locality previously but slightly affected. On Xmas day
a chasm 30ft across at surface appeared on the hanging-
wall side of the lode, within a few feet of the old railway
viaduct lately dismantled. The caved-in ground has, at
present, fallen about 15ft., and the sides of the cavity
overhang at a stiff angle—evidence that the disturbed
area is much greater below than at surface. The timbers
below have fared badly. The divergent railway which
replaces the old viaduct over this portion of the mine was
finished none too soon.
Gnashing of teeth amongst B.H. Proprietary
officials over the rumoured appointment of Uriah Dudley,
secretary of the Australasian Institute of Mining Engi-
neers, and an old-time Barrier mine-manager, to the
management of the lately-completed chloridising works.
The post was reckoned a safe thing for some one of the
mine staff, and the appointment of an outsider was a
possibility never dreamt of.

• • •
The lately-discovered “ Sneaker’s Gully ” field,
near Rockhampton (Q.), got its name from the prospectors,
who had long been working “on thequiet." On theirreturn
from a tucker-buying trip they found the storekeeper had
taken up ground next to them. The dodge of striking
their tent and pretending to clear out had no effect. To
relieve their feelings they named the field “Sneaker’s
Gully.” So far the two prospectors have taken £llOO out
of their claim.

When are the Kapai-Vermonb crowd in Sydney
going to pull themselves together? Honibrooke, the
manager, reports, week after week, that he has a big
lode showing good gold from wall to wall. Several good
trial crushings have been made, and everything seems
ready for a regular and large output; but previous dis-
appointments with the Great Mercury have sickened the
machinery calls.
# * #
The cyanide patentees are nob going to have it
all their own way. In several parts of the world mines
have used the process and ignored the patentees’ right to
royalty ; and now a combination of South African mining
men are going to test the validity of the patent. Apart
from the question of patent right, the co. has received big
diva, out of royalties which it will probably never
refund. The use of cyanide as a gold-solvent was prac-
tically unknown, and for this the co. deserves what it
has made out of it.
* « •
Mount Morgan sends its slimes away for treat-
ment. £37,000 worth of gold was got from this source
uring last half-year.
# # •
N.Q. papers describe Towalla, a scrub district
at the back of Geraldton (Johnston River, Q,), as “a poor
man’s goldfield." It is more—it soon makes a rich man
poor. “ Two small reefs, averaging 30z.” (writes a Taralla
resident); “some surface shows carrying gold for about
3ft. and nothing below that. Men who, deceived by N.Q.
paper accounts, came here, are now padding the hoof
through the country, looking for work, or else gully-
raking chasing the nimble pennyweight, said pennyweight
is worth 35.; average, Idwt. a day,”
# * 9
The obstinate old Balmain Ferry Co., it must
be admitted, has, so far, been simply slaughtered by the
new co-operative co. The old ferry authorities, though
paramount in the Port Jackson s.s. Co., seemed to learn
nothing from their bitter experience, and now it is evident
that the Manly Ferry Co. has come to stay. The advent
of the newco., to whom it was due that 20,000 people
visited Manly cn a recent day. means a little fortune for
the shopkeepers end publicans. The crowd drank all one
publican’s beer before mid-day on Boxing Day.

* # •
Wool is now being conveyed from Forbes
(N.S.W.) to Sydney per team, in preference to rail. The
first set of teamsters took 12 tons, and expected, with
return loading, to clear £5O by the trip. The Railway
Commissioners are understood to be reconsidering their
rates.
• * »
The new find of shell at Ganthemne Point,
W.A., is a very rich one. Late news is that 60 luggers
and four schooners were all hard at work. The doing
ranges from 12 to 18 fathoms. Erickson’s schooner
secured the best of the many good pearls obtained—6l
grains, and valued at £4OO.

“ Hang me if this isn’t as bad as the con-
fessional ! ” yelled a Maorilander who wanted to

Robert Stout was quizzed by a woman, at
an election-meeting, for being a freethinker.
Inquiry discovered that she was living with a man
—not her husband. So, when she came a second
time on the warpath, Stout asked her name. She
didn’t give her husband’s name, and “I thought
you were Mrs. —— ? ” sent her out for fresh air.
The foundering of the steamer Alert near
Sorrento, and the loss of 15 lives should be taken
(but won’t) as a warning by the owners and cap-
tains of scores of N.S.W. coasting-steamers. Any
day, small steamers may be seen plunging labori-
ously along, weighted down with an amount of
cargo that would, even in smooth water, render the
steamer noticeable, and carrying in their stuffy
little cabins as many as 20 or 30 passengers. Often
enough the hatches are off and sometimes a lump
of a sea may flop down and fill the for’ard well-
deck ; but provided no water gets into the hold
it is thought nothing of. Yet the skipper and
others are quite aware that if two or three seas
tumbled over in quick succession and theshipfailed
to free herself another might make its way into
the stoke-hole and send the ship down like a
stone.
The story of Wood, M.L.A., of South
Australia, seems to grow more complicated every
time that gentleman is heard of. At latest
advices the tale of his difficulties was something
like this : Wood married in England at an early
age, and, after a while, he and his wife separated.
He married again, and so did she, therefore he
seems to have two wives living, while she has two
husbands. Wood, however, claims that his first
wife had a husband living when he married her,
so that his first marriage was null and void, and
his second one holds good. Meanwhile, the lady
denies the existence of the alleged first husband,
so the question is, whether Wood committed
bigamy, or whether the alleged Mrs, Wood com-
mitted trigamy, or more? Meanwhile, Wood’s
constituents have decided, with much applause,
that Wood is worthy of their fullest confidence,
and there the matter rests for the present.
True copy of a “Merry Xmaa” letter that
has been shown to The Bulletin “ Bathurst,
Dec. 29, 1893. Dear Joe, —I am writing you a
few lines to let you know how I am. Well, the
truth I must tell. I have not done a tap since I
left home, only travelling about looking for a job,
but in vain. I put off writing to you, thinking I
would drop into something and be able to send
home some money, but it is the other way about.
I am here in Bathurst, barefooted, and don’t know
where to go and ask for a pair of boots. The
people tell me they are asked every day by men
for something or other, and that they cannot give
any more. I want to ask you, Joe, please, if you
could manage to send me as much as would get
me a pair of boots and a bit of food, and, as soon
as I can earn it, the first money you will get. I
know it is hard to ask it, but I held back as long
as I could. I have seen starvation this trip, I
must have travelled over 2000 miles and got
nothing. At the present time I am stopping in
the bush and begging food. . . P.S.—I hope
you all had a merry Xmas, which I had not. A
Happy New Year to you all.”—
Send for Prince George!
Let’s all have a gorge,
Turkeys and sucking-pigs carving;
Spend thousands on ‘‘May,’*
And be ready to pay
Kicks to our own people starving.
In the opinion of the most experienced of
the Newcastle coal-mining engineers and experts a
colliery at Cremorne ? Sydney Harbour, has but a
small chance of being established and a still
smaller one of being worked profitably should it be
established. Old Harry Harper, perhaps one of
the most experienced coal exploiters in the world,
says that from first to last the expenses will be
colossal and will greatly outweigh the advantages
of position. He asserts that the coal will be even
better than the bore shows—experience teaches
that the thicker the cover the better the quality of
the mineral. The average cost of sinking under the
most favourable circumstances is estimated at £35 a
foot and the average rate about ten feet a week, so
that the cost of sinking the two shafts alone will be
over a quarter of a million and the time occupied
about six years. Then perfect winding machinery
of the most costly sort must be used, and ventilat-
ing fans capable of throwing far more air into the
mine than any ever yet constructed. Should any
largo body of water be met with the expenses of
shafting will almost be doubled. It is his opinion
that the seam struck is identical with the upper
one of the Newcastle measures which he has
traced as far south as Bird Island. There it
dips under the Hawkesbury sandstone, the forma-
tion which is superimposed on the Cremorne seam.
He also hazards the opinion that should the bore
be sent down another hundred feet it will pierce
the other two seams of the Newcastle fields. The
temperature is of but little practical account except
inasmuch as it will cause a slight increase in
working-expenses. Mr. Croudace, manager of the
Scottish Australian Co.’s mines, says that the
tremendous pressure on the coal will greatly in-
crease the cost of timbering. Instead of costing
about 4d. a ton to timber, as in Newcastle, the
expense may be as high as 2s. a ton, and only
the most skilful and experienced miners can be
employed on that duty. Moreover the coal, for
the same reason, will have to be worked on that
expensive and wasteful method known as the
"long-wall system.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The cricket-matoh, N.S. Wales v. South Aus-
tralia, ended in an easy victory for the S.A. men, with
237 runs to the good. In their first innings the Adelaide
crowd scored 106, and the Cornstalks followed with 118.
Then the South Australians made a stupendous effort and
compiled 483 in their second innings, a result chiefly
owing to the splendid play and good luck of George
Gillen, who held his wicket for 6i hours and scored 205
runs. Lyons made 82, and the next highest score was
Jarvis’ 30. N.S. Wales then went to the wickets two
men short, Donnan and Pierce being on the sick list, and
made a total of 234, of which Callaway scored 71; Iredale,
41; and Bannerman a humble 1.
# # •
N.S.W. had another stroke of bad luck in the
match against the Victorian cricketers, who won by
three wickets. In the first innings Moses’ 71 and
Iredale's 27 were the chief elements in the score of 155.
Turner, Murdoch, Callaway, and Mackenzie registered a
duck’s-egg apiece. Victoria followed with 126, and no
sensational scores. N.S. Wales’ second innings closed for
174, Murdoch, with 64, being the principal contributor.
Then the Victorians rapidly compiled 204 for seven
wickets down, Graham, with a score of 68, and Trott
with 54, being responsible for the best part of the
result. The gate-money was £1283, a painful falling-
off of nearly £7OO as compared with last year.
• * *
Vie. Cricketing Ass. already (!) discusses who’s
to manage the next English tour, and Almanac Scott
has thrown in his caber.
* * #
The way is being paved for the Parsee cricketers,
in Australia.
« * •
“ Bob Bets ” have scandalised the Melb. metal-
lician world. A lot of petty bookmakers were “ lum-
bered ” by the police the other day, at Richmond Pony
Races. A policeman said they were making threepenny
bets, with women and boys.
• * *
George Giffen is to be the leader in making
charges against Manager Cohen, before the Cricket
Council. It is alleged that Giffen and Lyons, especially
the former, have a big money grievance.
» # •
The great George Giffen could not be induced
to play for his club —the Norwoods—in Adelaide the
other day. During Giffen’s absence he has been dis-
placed both as a selector of S.A. teams and as captain of
his club.

How came Jeweller to lose the Caulfield Cup ?

year-old race at Caulfield on Boxing Day. Tom Hales
bought the filly as a yearling.

He the Australian Eleven fracas, it is strange
that the young members of the team were the only ones
who didn’t pull any noses, row with the manager, &c.
Gregory, Trumble, and Graham, instead of taking an ex-
ample from Blaokham and Giffen, appear to have set
the older men one.
# # #
The lightning fighter, Joe Goddard, has been
beaten again—by Laing, of Maoriland, in a £l5O match of
20 rounds, whereof 12 only were fought. Goddard played
cautiously instead of in his old whirlwind style, and
though at times he held a strong advantage, after all the
tricky Laing fairly wore him down. Goddard alleges
“ lack of condition,” but then he was “ out of condition ”
when he succumbed to Denver Smith. The man who is
always out of condition has lost his condition. Goddard
will be a bright exception to the general rule if he doesn’t
now leave the ring and get into condition again bypu'Ung
beer for the noble Australian public.
In general terms, the old agreement (1893)
between the Northern District coal-owners and
the miners was, that the wage of the latter should
be 3s. 6d. a ton when the selling price of coal was
9s. a ton, and that every rise or fall of Is. in the
Belling price should be accompanied by a rise or
fall of 4d. per ton in the hewing rate. During
1893 the selling price was 9s. a ton, and so small
were the earnings of the men that, in a general,
unofficial way, they had determined to resist any
further reduction whatever. For the last four
-months most of the coal companies were, without
apparent cause, gradually reducing the number of
men in their employ. This “ cavilling out,” as it
is called, caused a feeling of defiant uneasiness
among the men, who surmised it was intended to
render them financially unfit to sustain a strike.
Towards the end of December the masters decided
to lower the selling price to Bs. per ton free on
board. Contemporaneously with the publication
of this decision, it was announced that the A. A.
Co., formerly the strongest supporter of the
Masters’ Association, had, in disgust, seceded
from that capitalistic mob, and alone intended to
adhere to the old agreement (3s. 2d. a ton), while
all the others posted notices at the pitheads,
stating their intention of paying only 2s. 10d,, in
that way shifting Bd. out of the shilling fall on to
the miners. On balloting, the majority of the
men decided to accept the A. A. Co.’s terms, but
with scornful unanimity rejected all others. The
iminers were masters of the situation, and had
a strike eventuated, it would have been the
.first time in the history of these disputes in
which the men had a positive assurance of
success, for the A.A. Co. by itself could
supply the whole trade of the port. At
(the last moment, however, the owners gave
way. Reviewing the actions of the capitalists in
tthis affair —the much-published defection of the
A.A. Co. from the Masters’ Ass., their com-
paratively liberal treatment of the men, and the
blatantly determined attitude of the other mine-
owners (who, one and all, declared that they
would rather lay their pits idle than pay 3s. 2d. a
ton), one cannot but conclude that the whole busi-
ness was a cunning, knavish trick intended to
jockey the men into accepting 3s. 2d. a ton all
round, a reduction they might otherwise have been
indisposed lu submit to.

Wool.—As is usual at this time of the year the sales
have been postponed for a fortnight, and our next will be
held on the 11th inst., when we will bring forward a very
attractive catalogue. There is a good private demand
existing for all classes at late rates.

At Coolgardie, of late, £1 has often been
given for water enough to satisfy a pair of buggy
horses.

from Brisbane (Q.), Weather-prophet Wragge’s
self-registering thermometer recorded 106.3 deg.
in the shade.

Some time ago a S.A. politician was
carving a Xmas duck or something, when his
knife slipped, and he thought he was going to die
from lockjaw. Wherefore he rushed to the bosom
of Mother Church, and is now known amongst his
enemies as “ the Carving-knife Catholic.”
One Coolgardie man saw so little fluid that
he never had a wash for six months. His luck
was out, too. While he was coming back to spend
ajdisgusted Christinas with his Adelaide friends,
he got wet to the skin by a succession of thunder-
storms, and his old mate struck one of the richest
shows yet chronicled.
N.S. Wales finishes 1893 with a surplus of
.£16,000 a small thing in itself yet something to
rejoice over as being the first real surplus the dis-
tressful province has seen for a good many years.
The year’s expenditure shows a reduction of
•£769,000, the result of much retrenchment and
hard economy on the part of the Dibbs Cabinet.
All sorts and conditions of men can be
found at Coolgardie. Broadnbb, the ex-Broken-
hillionaire and 3600-guinea-Titan-gelding man
was there a few weeks ago. There is another man
there who was once a member of the Four-in-hand
Club, and has been seen driving the Prince of
Wales behind a spanking tandem team. He was
dry-blowing on Hannan’s rush.
And are we to assume that even the
Radical S.A. Premier Kingston joined in inviting
York and May to Australia—instead of seizing
the opportunity to sneer at the hideous alleged
English of J. B. Patterson’s circular, and the
greasy toadyism which alone could prompt such a
scheme at a time when our people are dying for
want of work? The correspondence in this matter
should be moved for.
Prendergast, the shooter of Mayor Harrison
of Chicago, was the author of a plan by which the
Chicago railroad companies would be compelled to
elevate their tracks and the outrageous loss of life
caused by the 1500 open and unprotected crossings
be reduced to a minimum. Harrison was bitterly
opposed to the proposed innovation, although, in
the first seven months of last year, 120 persons
were killed on these crossings by passing trains.
The wildly eulogistic notices of Harrison never
alluded to the fact of his being the nominee and
advocate of the gambling and liquor interests of
the hog city.
Messrs. Fisher and Lingham, dealers in
National Cash Registers and Smith Premier
Typewriters, forward a mass of most interesting
and charmingly-printed literature dealing with
their specialities. Among them is a massive book
containing 1001 letters expressive of the writers’
cordial approval of the National Cash Register, for
its accuracy, simplicity, and cheapness, and the
strict honesty which distinguishes it from the old
style of live meat cash-keeper. Also it can be
made to register credit sales as well as cash, and
to do most of the book-keeping in a small estab-
lishment. The Smith Typewriter is represented
by a number of letters written with that imple-
ment, and remarkable for neatness and absolute
freedom from defect. The American firms which
manufacture these articles send out a mass of
marvellously elegant illustrated books concerning
them.

AN XMAS TALE.
Dear Bulletin,—l charge
You tell it not to all.
My heart—it was so large ;
My means—they were so small.
Had I been Croesus? Whoo !
I would have sent no end
Of Xmas Presents to
Each enemy and friend.
My heart and soul they ache
Because of this, my need ;
I hope my fiieuds will take
The will as for the deed.
But, could I let it go,
L ; ke broker’s pledges, by
Without one gift to show ?
I couldn’t. No; not I!
Ah ! season eloquent
Of joy and festive larks !
I couldn’t. So I sent
An old dead cat to Parkes.
THE DIPSOMANIAC.
Sydney’s advance in the matter of
morality is one signal incident of the depression,
and landlords whose tenants comprised the
“widow lady who wants to borrow .£s’’from
kind but hairless gent., and the lady whose hus-
band is away in the country, find that the demand
for conveniently-situated premises has fallen away
to nothing. Tne police explain that many of the
undesirables have gone Coolgardie way, and the
rest to Paraguay.
An American critic, writing in one of the
magazines, says that h« has seen English “women
of the best class struck by stones and eggs and
dead fish ” while electioneering on behalf of their
friends or relatives. He omits to state whether
the eggs and fish were also of the best class, but
no doubt they were of the best class for electioneer-
ing purposes. It is the Primrose dames who
•come in for these favours, the British plutocracy
having a finely admiring eye for the lower classes
when polling day is on.
■Colemane & Sons’ Eucalypte Pills stir one up.
When a Hebrew dies his body is taken
charge of by officials from the synagDgue and
buried in a plain, deal coffin—rich and poor alike.
“ We brought nothing into this world, and it is
certain we can carry nothing out and fond as
Jews are of show they bow to precept. But for
the number of be-diamonded “followers”it would
be difficult to distinguish between a millionaire
Hebrew’s funeral and a pauper’s, buried at the
State’s expense. It is only the Gentile who takes a
ghoulish delight in having a pompous burial,
with all its dismal paraphernalia, gin-besotted
hangers-on, and ruinous expense.
Members of the Melbourne W.C.T.U.
urge the Government to abolish the use of penny-
in the-slot cigarette machines, as they are a
temptation to the small boy with a penny for the
missionaries. The W.C.T.U. would be content if
the mechanism of the machine were so altered as
to deliver a tract on the evils of tobacco to the
youngster who expected a cigarette. The langu ge
of the average small boy under these cir-
cumstances, however, would not be good for
W.C.T.U. ears, but if the machine could be so
fixed as to promptly vomit forth another tract on
the awful sinfulness of profane language it would
just about meet the case.

A Sinner’s Comedy.
Epigrams, Aphorisms and Things, Stolen for
“The Bulletin ” from John Oliver Hobbes’
“Sinner’s Comedy” by M.L.M.
In life there are no Unities, but three Incom-
preheusibles : Destiny, Man, and Woman.
* * •
Just before he died he kissed his wife’s hand
and ca'led her “Elizabeth.” She had been chris-
tened Augusta Frederica; but then, as the doctors
explained, dying men often make these mistakes.
« # «
Men heap together the mistakes of their lives,
and create a monster which they call Destiny.
• * *
It is only a very unselfish man who cares to be
loved. The majority prefer to love—it lays them
under fewer obligations,
* * *
When a man suspects that his God is not taking
him seriously he changes his religion,
* * *
To a young girl marriage means only a trous-
seau and a honeymoon ; she imagines the honey-
moon as a flirtation under the blessing of the
Church.
« • »
Emily was shocked at his death and grieved
because she could not grieve. He had been so
very kind and so very stupid. She went in
mournful weeds,, and ordered orchids to be placed
on bis grave twice a week. Her mother suggested
—“At all events, for the present.”
m * *
“To see men as they are not, and never could
be, is the privilege peculiar to the feminine nature.
You see,” he went on, “love comes to man
through his senses—to woman through her im-
agination. I might even say, taking the subject
on broad lines, that women love men for their
virtue; while men, very often, love women for the
absence of it.”
* # *
When a man gets an idea into his head about a
woman, whatever she may say or do only gives
him one more reason for sticking to it. It is only
when he gets an equally strong idea about some
other subject or some other woman that he becomes
nicely critical.
* * *
No man knows his language till he has lived it.
• • #
The secret of managing a man is to let him
have his own way in little things. He will change
his plan of life when he won’t change his boot-
maker.
• • •
Women have boundless faith in the sobering
effect of Commonplace. It is the remedy they
administer to disordered passions.
« * *
“A man may give his whole life to a woman,
and it won’t mean so much to her as if he had once
jawed her out of neuralgia.” “And a woman,”
said Emily, “ may give her soul for a man, and he
won’t think so much of her as if she had jilted
him for somebody else.”
• • *
“My wife was beautiful,” he said; “I cannot
tell how beautiful.” For the lover there is only
one glory. He paused and sighed ; his eyes seemed
to pierce into another world. . . . “Fancy!”
said Mrs. Grimmage, “only fancy! Was she
very nice?” . * . “Nice? Dear God! Where
did you learn that word? Nice?” He threw
back his head and laughed.
• • *
“Work is good,” murmured Legge, “the only
good—except Hope. I have lots of Hope.” ....
“Oh, yes,” said Anna, “there is Hope.” She
looked hopeless. ... I have been harder hit
than you,” said Legge. “I died twelve years
ago; the only thing about me that lives is my
stomach. I remember they fed it with chops—on
the day she was buried.”
* * •
He remembered her as one remembers some
certain night in Jiine—that it was perfect for
June—that a year of such would be unhealthy.
* # *
He shaved regularly for the week following
Lord Middlehurst’s death, gave his lounging-coat
to an under-groom, and began reading religious
novels, in bed, as a first step towards reform. At
the end of the tenth day he hinted to the coach-
man that a rat-hunt might be amusing,
# # #
The tableaux. Emily, as “ Vivien,” under
limelight, showed him that Emily was frivolous;
also, that her hair fell below her waist. Both dis-
coveries were soul-plaguing: the first because it
jarred; the second because he shared it with
assembled Middlehurst. After the performance
he was the only one who did not offer some
tribute to her beauty. “7 am afraid,” she said,
“you don't care for Tennyson.”
• • •
He reflected that some women were like melo-
dies. The indefinite “ some women ”is an inspira-
tion which comes to every man in his hour of
peril.
* • •
Solitude d deux makes solitude only one of two
things—perfect rest or complete destruction.
* # #
One of the gentlemen wore the gaiters of a
dean. One of the ladies looked as though she
would like to wear them, if only for half-an-hour.
She was a small woman, and extremely slight, yet,
in spite of her slimness, there was not the faintest
sign of bone about her; in fact, it was said that
she had not a bone in her body. She was com-
posed of flesh, blood, and spirit.
Mr. Digby Yallence was a gentleman of some
fame who had translated Theocritus out of
honesty into English, discovered a humourist in
Jeremy Taylor, damned Rousseau, and, in his
leisure, bred canaries. Mrs. Digby was tall o’
span, with a small face, big eyes, and a large
mouth. Digby was fond of saying that his wife’s
face was geometrically impossible. The parts were
greater than the whole.
* • * *
The Dean’s sister was the widow of an Irish
major, who had left her his lame hunter, four very
healthy little boys, and a dying command that
she should do her duty by the children.
Sacheverell awoke one morning to find the pitiful
group on his doorstep in St Thomas’s-in-the-
Lkufes, where he held a small living. “I knew
you would be glad to have us,” said Eleanor.
The next day his study was referred to as the
drawing-room, and he was moved away to the
attic out of the children’s noise.
» # *
The great law of infidelity in the human race.
A man must be faithless to something—either to
a woman, or his God, or his firmest belief.
* * *
All is vanity, and discovering it—the greatest
vanity.
# • *
“You can’t command the Future,” said
Carlotta. “Let me think I can,” said Emily.
“That’s half the battle.”
* # #
“You are very beautiful in anything,” he said.
“If you think so,” she answered, as gravely, “it
will make me beautiful.”
• • •
• If the gods have no sense of humour they must
weep a great deal.

treatment for Piles, consult Mr. A. Sharp on the

A Queensland civil servant recently
regretted his “inability to attend” at the office
on account of the serious illness of his “god-
father.” Which indistinctly reminds The Bul-
letin of another public servant in one of the
Australias who was accused of getting a week’s
leave because he wanted to colour a new meer-
schaum.


The New City Bank of
Sydney Premises.
No more artistically conceived and worthier
example of the abilities, skill and experience of
the designers, constructors and other craftsmen
employed on the many palatial public buildings
that ornament the city of Sydney, is to be seen
than that which has been achieved by the erectors
of the new premises of the City Bank of Sydney,
shown hi the accompanying illustration. This
structure takes first rank among the many magni-
THE NEW CITY BANK OF SYDNEY, PITT STREET, SYDNEY.

the chief glory of the Queen City of the South.
the site-and is devoted entirely to the business of
are in perfect accord with its stateliness of
elevation. The Pyrmont stone fagade has an
altitude of 78ft., surmounted by a graceful tower,
which rises to a height of 110 ft. The spandrels
display chastely-executed sculptures represent-
ing* Manufactures and Commerce (each de-
picted by a female figure in a reclining attitude),
Justice, and Telegraphy; and Agriculture, Navi-
gation, Mining, and Minting—these four latter
being pourtrayed by boys working at their respec-
tive avocations.
That portion of the building facing Pitt-street,
shown in the fagade of our illustration, consists,
above the ground-floor, of a mezzanine and four

features of the building, the vestibule is specially
worthy of a few words. Entering from Pitt-

the beholder. On either side handsornely-orna-

open into thegeneral banking chamberinfront. The
sides of the vestibule are rich in their linings with
the choicest and rarest marbles of the Pyrenees—
Br&ohe Ombre, Grande Antique, Rouge Griotte,
and the lovely Yert de Alpes. Overhead, the
ceiling of Sicilian marble, coffered with enriched
bronze panels, and supported by a rich cornice,
forms one of the many beauties of the vestibule,
whioh is a work of beauty and taste throughout.
The new bank claims to have, and does possess,
in the large banking chamber the most magnifi-
cent room in Australasia devoted to banking
purposes, and no written description of it could
be attempted but that would fail to do justice to
it in many particulars. Imagine, however, a
chamber 100 feet in length, nearly 43 feet
in width, and 27 feet in height from floor to
ceiling, divided into three main compartments
by detached Corinthian columns and pilasters
placed upon a stylobate, and supporting the great
principal beams. In the centre compartment is a
domed skylight 24ft. in diameter, rising’ to a
height of 46ft. from the floor, and glazed with
stained glass similar to that of the windows, with
lateral and spandrel ceiling panels. Although
this scant outline of the general banking chamber
may convey a faint idea of its main features—its
size and noble proportions generally—no idea can
be gained, except by a personal inspection, of the
wealth of decoration, the beauty of colour, and the
all-round sumptuousness that are manifested
wherever the eye may rest.
The general bank accommodation may be briefly
outlined by saying that in the splendidly-lighted
basement are three additional strong rooms,
clerks’ luncheon-room—3oft. x 18ft.—store-room
for stationery, messenger’s room, the electric-
lighting plant and the furnace for the destruction
of old bank-notes, while in the rear is further

A Tale of a Mask.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Lannigan, who lived on Motukoe, was in
debt to his firm. This was partly due to his fond-
ness for trade gin and partly because Bully
Hayes had called at the island a month or so back
and the genial Bully and he had played a game or
two of poker.
“ I’ll give you your revenge when I come back
from the Carolines, Lannigan,” said the redoubt,
able captain as he scooped in every dollar of the
trader’s takings for the past six months. And
Lannigan, grasping his hand warmly and declar-
ing it was a pleasure to be “claned out by a
gintleman,” bade him good-bye and went to sleep
away from home for a day with some native
friends. Tariro, his Manhiki wife, had a some-
what violent temper, and during the poker
incident had indulged in much vituperative
language outside, directed at white men in general
and Lannigan in particular.
• • •
“ See, thou swiller of gin, see what thy
folly has brought us to,” said the justly-incensed
Tariro, when he came back, and with her took
stock of his trade goods; “ a thousand and fiv e
hundred dollars’ worth of trade came we here
with, and thou hast naught to show for it but
five casks of oil and a few stinking shark-fins 5
and surely the ship of the m alo (his firm) will be
here this month.”
Lannigan was in a bit of a fix. The firm he was
trading for on Motukoe didn’t do business in the
same free-and-easy way as did Bobby Towns’
captains and the unconventional Bully Hayes.
They made him sign papers, and every time the
ship came the rufous-headed Scotch supercargo
took stock, and a violent altercation would result
over the price of the trade; but as the trader
generally had a big lot of produce for the ship
matters always ended amicably. He—or rather
his wife, Tariro—was too good a trader to have an
open rupture with, and the wordy warfare always
resulted in the trader saying, in his matter-of-fact
way, “ Well, I suppose it’s right enough. You only
rob me wanst in twelve months, and I rob the
natives here every day of my life. Give ms in a
case of gin, an’ I’ll send ye a pig.”
* * •
But he had never been so much in debt as
he was now. Tariro and he talked it over, and hit
upon a plan. He was to say, when the ship came,
that he had but five casks of oil; all his trade had
been sold for cash, and the cash —a thousand
dollars—represented by a bag of copper bolts
picked up on the reef from an old wreck, was to
be taken off to the ship and accidentally dropped
overboard as it was being passed upon deck. This
was Lannigan’s idea, and Tariro straightway tied
up the bolts in readiness in many thicknesses of
sail-cloth.
* • •
“Here’s Lannigan coming,” called out
the captain of the trading vessel to the super-
cargo, a week or so afterwards, “ and that saucy
Manhiki woman as usual with him, to see that
he doesn’t get drunk. The devil take such as
her ! There’s no show of getting him tight.”
“How are you, Lannigan?” said the super-
cargo, wiping his perspiring brow. He had just
come out of the hold where he had been opening
tinned meats, and putting all the “blown” tins
he could find into one especial case—for Lannigan.
This was what he called “makin’ a mairgin for
loss on the meats, which didna’ pay well.”
“Eine,” said the genial Lannigan, “an’ I
haven’t got but five casks of oil for yez. Devil a
drop av oil would the people make when they
looked at the bewtiful lot av trade ye gave me
last time. They just rushed me wid cash, an’l
tuk a matter av a thousand dollars or so in a
month.
“Yerra guid business,” said the supercargo,
“ but ye made a gran’ meestake in selling the
guids for Cheelian dollars instead of oil. An’ sae
I must debit ye wi a loss of twenty-five par cent,
on the money— ”
“Chile dollars be damned 1” said Lannigan,
“all good American dollars—we've had about
twenty whaleships here, buyin’ pigs an’ poultry
an’ pearl shell.”
“Twenty-one ship,” said Tariro, blowing the
smoke of her cigarette through her pretty little
nose.
“ Whaur’s the money, onyway ?” said the
supercargo, “let’s get to business, Lannigan. Eh
mon, I’ve some verra fine beef for ye.”
“ Get the bag up out of the boat, Tariro,” said
the trader, “ it’s mighty frightened I was havin’
so much money in the house at wanst, wid all
them rowdy Yankee sailors from the whaleships
ashore here.”
• • •
There was a great crowd of natives on
deck —over a hundred—and the mate was swearing
violently at them for getting in his way. The
schooner was a very small vessel, and Motukoe
being her first place of call for cargo, she was in
light trim;, having only her trade and a little
ballast on board.
“Send those natives away from the galley,” he
called out to the cook, who was giving some of the
young women ship-biscuits in exchange for young
cocoanuts, “can’t you see the ship keeps flying up
in the wind with all those people for’ard !”
» « •
Hekemanu, Lannigan’s native “Man Jack,”
sat in the boat towing alongside, with the bag of
“ dollars ” at his feet. He and all the boat’s crew
were in the secret. Lannigan owned their souls;
besides, they all liked him on Motukoe.
Tariro stood for a moment beside the captain,
indulging in the usual broad “chaff,” and then
leaning over the rail she called out to Hekemanu :
Ta mai te taga tupe (“ give me the bag of
money.”)
The man for’ard hauled on the line to bring the
boat alongside the schooner, and Hekemanu stood
up with the heavy bag in his hand.
“ Hold on there, you fool. If you drop that bag
I’ll knock your head off,” said the skipper.
“Here, Mr. Bates, just you jump down and take
that money from that native or he’ll drop it, sure.”
Before Hekemanu had time to let it fall over the
side the mate had jumped into the boat and
taken it.
Lannigan, putting his head up out of the little
cabin, groaned inwardly as he saw the mate step
over the rail with the fateful bag and hand it to
the supercargo.
“ Be the powers, ye’re in a mighty hurry for the
money,” said Lannigan roughly, taking it from
him, “ye might ax me if I had a mouth on me
first.”
The supercargo laughed and put a bottle of gin
on the table, and Lannigan’s fertile brain com-
menced to work. If he could only get the super-
cargo out of the cabin for a minute he meant to
pick up the bag, and declaring he was insulted
get it back into his boat and tell him to come and
count it ashore. Then he could get capsized on
the reef and lose it. They were always having
“ barneys ’ and it would only be looked upon as
one of his usual freaks.
.
“What the deuce is that? he said,
pointing to a hideous, highly-coloured paper mask
that hung up in the cabin.
The supercargo handed it to him. “It’s for a
man in Samoa—a silly, joking body, always play-
ing pranks wi’ the natives, and I thoct he would
like the thing.”
“ Bedad, ’tis enough to scare the sowl out av
the divil, said Lannigan.
Just then a mob of natives came aft, and the
two men in the cabin heard the captain tell them
to clear out again. They were saucy and wouldn’t
go. Hekemanu had told them of the failure of
Lannigan’s dodge, and they had an idea that the
ship would take him away, and stood by to rescue
him at the word of command.
“ I’ll verra soon hunt them, said the super-
cargo, with a proud smile, and he put the mask
on his face. Tariro made a bolt on deck and called
out to the natives that the supercargo was going
to frighten theriTwith a mask.
# # »
Instead of wild yells of fear, and jumping
overboard, as he imagined would happen, the
natives merely laughed, but edged away for’ard.
The schooner was in quite close to the reef; the
water was very deep, and there was no danger of
striking. She was under jib and mainsail only,
but the breeze was fresh and she was travelling at a
great rate. The wind being right off the land, the
skipper was hugging the reel as closely as possible,
so as to bring up and anchor on a five-fathom
patch about a mile away.
“ Here, quit that fooling,” he called out to the
supercargo, “and come aft, you fellows. The
ship is that much down by the head she won t pay
off, with the helm hard up_.
One look at the crowd of natives and another at
the shore, and a wild idea came into Lannigan s
head. He whispered to. Tariro, who went up
for ard and said something to the natives. In
another ten seconds some of them began to
clamber out on the jibboom, the rest after them.
“ Come back 1 ” yelled the skipper, jamming the
helm hard up, as the schooner flew up into the
wind. “Leggo peak halyards. By G—d ! we
are running ashore. Leggo throat halyards, too.
The mate flew to the halyards, and let go first
the peak and then the throat halyards; but it was
too late, and, .with a swarm of natives packed
together for ard from the galley to the end of the
jibboom, she stuck her nose down, and, with
stern high out of the water, like a duck chasing
flies, she crashed into the reef—ran ashore dead to
No one was drowned. The natives took
good care of the captain, mate, and supercargo,
and helped them to save all they could. But
Lannigan had a heavy loss—the bag of copper
bolts had gone to the bottom.
The captain and supercargo had very strong
suspicions, but, as Lannigan was their host and
practically their ruler during the three months
they remained on Motukoe, they said nothing.
But they gave him a wide berth afterwards.
LOUIS BECHE.
N.S.W. country farmer* are paid 2|d.
per gallon for milk which is retailed in Sydney at
16d.
Colemane & Sons’ Eucalypte Ex. is the strongest.
Ever since millionaire Astor took over the
Pall Mall Gazette to “ get on to ” that paper
is the highest ambition of every London journalist
—every sordid one, anyway. The general pay
there is many per cent, more than elsewhere; and
if the matter is very good the salary is ladled out
with both hands. At first Astor fixed the amount
to be “ lost ” at £12,000 a-year, but the loss
threatens to overstep J 120,000. Which, altogether,
is a goodly sum to pay for the privilege of living
in a foreign country and buttressing a throne.
Colemane & Sons’ Eucal. Loz. are for the voice.
“Jim Griffin’s horse ” has “ revolushed ”
Northern Tasmania. Years ago, when the
Launceston-Deloraine railway was proposed, the
farmers along the route promised, if the line
didn’t pay interest on cost of construction, to
submit to a railway tax. The tax was imposed,
but, in the two decades or so of interval, the
farmers changed their minds, and now refuse to
pay. Then “Jim Griffin’s horse” was seized for
arrears of tax and auctioned at Deloraine. Not a
solitary bid. Then at Westbury. Same result.
So the animal was removed to Launceston and
advertised for sale on a certain date. By that
morning’s early train came down “Griffinites”
galore. They paraded the streets, carrying a
large coffin, bearing on each side the legend :
“Eor the man that buys Jim Griffin’s horse.”
Nobody wanted to inhabit the coffin, and the
“ lot ” remained unsold. And at latest advices
“ Jim Griffin’s horse ” was still going to and fro
on the face of the earth in a vain search for a pur-
chaser.
Colemane & Sons’ Yic. Ointment is marvellous
The barque Loongana brings news from
the Gilbert Islands that the three years drought
still continues and the traders are reduced to the
awful necessity of drinking neat gin (sold to them
at 500 per cent profit) instead of the temperance
cocoanut. A private letter to a Sydney resident
from a trader at Butatitari states that the white
residents, who, in the main, howled enthusias-
tically over the annexation of the group, have been
disenchanted of the glory of living under British
rule by High Commissioner Thurston, who has in-
terfered with many of their little privileges to an
alarming extent. According to the letter, Thurston
“ is made a god of by the native missionaries and
sucks in all they tell him like a sponge. ” The
writer adds, more in sorrow than in anger : “ We
will have to knuckle down to the native teachers
now with a vengeance or get yanked off the islands
as undesirable residents. 1 went on board the
Sydney trading steamer the other day and when
Igothomemy wife told me that the native teacher’s
wife had been in and asked her to find out if
my breath smelt of gin, so that I could be fined.
Hard, isn’t it ? (Think of a white man being offi-
cially smelt by a saved Kanaka —the ignominy of
it). . , . The Yankee firm have sold out to
the Dutchmen at Jaluit and now there is nothing
for us poor devils to ,do but get goods from the
Sydney firms and bilk them instead of helping to
bilk the natives.”

A Bornean Lover.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Stubbs, the “stick-hunter,” jumped out
of his hammock. “Hold on, Dan ! I know
where you’re qff to, and what I say is—don’t go!”
Dan Dickson turned short round with the door-
screen raised in his hand. “Look here, my little
man 1 you’ve been so long pottering about after
mashing-sticks for your London Chollies and
Johnnies that you’ve lost what little nerve you
Cockneys ever have!”
Little Stubbs grinned good-naturedly at the
notion. “ Walking-canes may be trifles, my lad,
but wild men and wild beasts ain’t trifles—nor
fever and dysentery, pizen-plants and pizen-rep-
tiles. And them’s the little chances the stick-
hunter’s got to take. Just you wait, my bold
Bananalander, till you’ve been as long in this here
bloomin’ Borneo ”
But the young Queenslander was off. Im-
patiently disdaining the ladder, he swung his
lanky six feet of carcase off the platform by his
hands and vanished amongst the fan-palms.
“Down river !” said Stubbs, looking after him.
“ It’s old Saji’s daughter he’s going to meet, Mr.
Beekley.”
“ Bad ! ” said the naturalist, glowering over his
spectacles.
“ And she’s promised to Tanga’s son—Tanga of
Bruti Island. Bao-Lu, the fellow’s name is, and,
as he’s hunted heads for the girl, this is like to be
a parang-business, or worse ! It ain’t here as it is
up Brunei way or over near the Dutch settle-
ments—these chaps won’t stand any dashed non-
sense about their women. If Dan won’t hear
reason, Dan’s a goner—and that’s all about it! ”
Out of a fragrant undergrowth—frangi-
panis and azaleas —stood up, slim and solitary, a
scarlet-crowned sterculia. Dan caught its blaze
against the sapphire sky, and halted cautiously
within the jungle-margin.
“There’s the red-headed tree—that’s the place !
And the sun’s abreast of the bamboo-tops—that’s
the time ! But where’s Balasi ? ” And then
Balasi answered for herself, creeping wild-eyed
out of the blossomy tangle, and pointing him
imperatively back by the way he had come.
He didn’t understand —or wouldn’t! “That
be hanged !” he muttered. “Now, I’m here, I’m
not going back without a kiss ! ” And, with the
words, a smacking kiss he got, as a rotten mud-
fish, aimed skillfully from the bamboo-clump,
landed square on his mouth and plastered him to
the eyes with greasy abomination.
Balasi fled squawking out amongst the man-
groves of the river; Dan clawed the stuff from
his eyes, whipped out his Smith-and-Wesson, and
glared every way but the right one, for, from
behind, a swift hand gripped him by the ankle,
and down he went upon his nose ! A knee was
on his pistol-arm in a moment, but hardly was the
weapon wrenched away before the Queenslander
was on his feet again—a sinewy twelve-stone of
astonished fury. But the Dyak—weight tor
weight—is probably the strongest man in all the
world. Two nines, or thereabouts, gave the white
man six stone the worst of it, and down he went
again, with a jabbering little yellow-skin on each
elbow. Round leg and arm went then the rapid,
lithe rattan—and Dan was done !
Struggling no more, he looked up at his captors.
Naked to the loin-cloth, bareheaded, weaponless
—these two saddle-coloured rascals couldn’t be
jealous, at any rate; for Dyak pride of birth
is mighty, and Balasi was a chief’s niece.
But then from the bamboos issued a resplendent
figure, and Dan understood. This must be Bao-
Lu; for Bao—like the professional assassin else-
where—was an exquisite of the first water. About
his conical rattan hat stood up a row of sun-bird
feathers, crimson and yellow; crimson, too, and
yellow his silk sarong; uncountable the graduated
rings—from finger-size to bracelet—that fringed
his ears around. His ihlang-sheath was bedight
with green-dyed human hair; his cloak was of
mias-skin, with grinning mask attached ; his tur-
meric-tinted hide was like brand-new pigskin;
and like night the blackness of his sinka-stained
teeth. Beneathhis turned-up nosegrew that dearly-
coveted Dyak distinction—a twenty-haired mous-
tache.
This gorgeous vision gazed for a minute
on the prostrate white man. Then it spoke—first,
Malay, which to Dan was Hebrew ; Dyak, then —
and here and there the captive caught a word he
knew. Something about “blood” he gathered,
and a “swamp,” and “water;” then two more
Dyaks trotted up, and Dan’s heart stood still a
moment as, at a word from the chief, the four men
hoisted him, log-like, on their shoulders. “ The
swamp?” he thought. “The crocodiles, of
course?” and, at the notion, with a frantic jerk
he rolled his trussed-up body clean over the heads
of the two men on one side of him. But rattan
lashings hold like steel; the Dyaks laughed, picked
him up again, and went on.
* • •
The sun went down in a lurid haze ; every
leaf was still as cast-iron; the white cranes
mourned and the trumpet-frogs jubilated, as the
swift tropic night marched over from Celebes.
Beekley went to his barometer. “Blow from the
westward before long !” he said, and, even as he
spoke, the far-away screech of the Sumatra came
faintly over the blackening ocean. Two minutes
more, and the top-knot of the lofty tappan before
the house was thrashing the reeds out of the
thatch—there was a shriek as of a thousand buzz-
saws—a fusillade of branches and pebbles—and the
squall had gone yelling away into the east. Then
both men started, as, out of the tomb-like succeed-
ing silence, there arose a sudden cry—weird and
inarticulate, but human.
“Dan!” said the stick-hunter, and out he
rushed upon the platform. Nobody there; but
again came the voice—from 'below. Beokley
brought out the lamp, held it over, and saw a
ghastly face. “Take the light!” he said, and
went down.
At the ladder-foot lay Dan, stripped to the
waist, and around him the ground was red with
something that smoked horribly in the lamp-
light. Between them they got him up the ladder;
Beekley gave him brandy, and Stubbs looked at
his wounds —all small, but all bleeding.
“What devil’s work is this?” said the stick-
hunter. “Thorns?”
“No ! ” said Beekley, “ leeches I See?”—and
he pointed to half-a-dozen of the yellow-striped
bloodsuckers still hard at work. “Not much
chance for the poor chap, I’m afraid ! But give
him some more brandy, and perhaps he’ll be able
to tell us something about this.”
The brandy did its work, and presently Dan
told them how, by Bao-Lu and company, he had
been stripped to the waist and dumped up to the
neck in a leech-swamp—fastened by the wrists to
an overhanging spiderwood. When the squall
came it snapped the branch, whereupon Dan had
struggled out and made for home, but had been
too far gone to get up the ladder.
When the sun came round again he found the
good-natured little Cockney blubbering freely over
a hole he was making under the shade of the
big tappan. Beekley came out and looked down
at him. “ That’ll do I” he said, softly. “ Won’t
be wanted, after all I ” Stubbs dropped his
chunkal and scurried up the ladder. “I knew
you were a slap-up doctor 1” he said. “ How did
you pull him through ?”
“It was Youth that pulled him through 1 Had
it been your case or mine, that hole would have
been wanted by this time ! ”
ALEX.MONTGOMERY.
FOUR HOURS TOO LATE.
“ Oh, Ood lif I could recall the past three or four
hours. £ee what trouble a man may bring upon
himself all in a moment .”
Mr. James Curtis and Mr. G. S. Bennett, both
of San Francisco, had been intimate friends for
years. Last summer they quarrelled for the first
time, each accusing the other of wrong. Better
thoughts prevailed and they were reconciled.
But you can’t undo what is once done. Memory
kept the record on her slate. They avoided each,
other as much as possible. Still, living as neigh-
bours, men must meet some time. These two met
in the street. The quarrel was renewed. Bitter
words flew fast and thick, and Curtis, beside him-
self with rage, drew his pistol and shot Bennett
dead on the spot. Later in the day he used the
above language—so laden with self-reproach and
sorrow. Yet how useless, how hopeless, how
vain.
But was Curtis right in saying that trouble may
come all in a moment ? True, it often seems so,
but is there not a deeper fact which we don’t see?
It matters nothing what the nature of the trouble
is. Therefore, let us consider a different case on
the same principle.
An intelligent woman says :—“ In October,
1890, I had an attack of illness from which I never
expected to recover. I had aching pains all over
me and a cough that nearly shook me to pieces.
I obtained no good sleep night or day, and had to
take to my bed. I was fed with liquid food from
an invalid’s cup, for I could not raise myself in
bed. My heart fluttered so you could hear it beat
on the pillow, and often pain struck through it as
though somebody had stabbed me. I lay perfectly
helpless and could scarcely breathe. A doctor
attended me over a month, but I grew weaker and
weaker. Sometimes at night I was so bad he
feared I would not live till morning. He called in
a consulting physician and both agreed that my
condition was critical. I was fed with brandy to
keep me alive. My husband and daughter stayed
with me almost constantly. None of the medicines
administered had any effect. I was almost at
death’s door,
“ At this time, Mrs. Keeling, of Mutley, near
Plymouth, a friend of mine, urged me to try a
medicine called Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup.
I procured a bottle, and after a few days I was
able to take and digest sufficient food to give me
some strength, and the worse symptoms were
greatly abated. After having used six bottles of
the Syrup my health was completely restored, and
I have since felt better than for the previous thirty
years. _ My two daughters have also been cured
of indigestion by it. I will gladly answer any
enquiries.”
(Signed) Mrs. Louise Jackson, Builders’Arms
Hotel, Bridge Road, Hammersmith, London,
Jan. 11th, 1892.
In the letter, from which the above is an extract,
Mrs. Jackson further states that for over twenty
years before the attack of October, 1890, she had
suffered from a disordered stomach and liver.
She had a bad taste in the mouth, a poor appetite,
and what little she ate gave her pain. She felt
dull, languid, and tired, and had a miserable
sinking feeling in the stomach, great pain in the
chest and sides, palpitation, giddiness, and fre-
quently fell in the street and had to be assisted
home.
So we perceive that in her case a cause, long in
operation, at last produced the crisis which came
near ending her life. It is always thus, whether
we recognise the cause or not. The crime com-
mitted by Curtis was the sudden passionate act of
a man who allowed thoughts of hate and vengeance
to take possession of his mind and breed the con-
dition which made murder possible. In the very
different case of this lady’s illness it was an enemy
of her body, indigestion and dyspepsia, which at
length broke out into violence.
The lesson is the same. Watch the beginning
of evil and check it while yet jit may be easily
controlled.

HIGHNESS THE
HIS MATE
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
h may have been a fragment of that higher
Truth dreams , at times , disclose ;
It may have been to Fond Illusion nigher —
But thus the story goes:
A fierce sun glared upon a gaunt land, stricken
With barrenness and thirst,
Where Nature’s pulse with joy of Spring would
quicken
No more ; a land accurst.
Grey salt-bush grimmer made the desolation—
Like mocking immortelles
Strewn on the graveyard of a perished nation
Whose name no record tells.
No faintest sign of distant water glimmered
The aching eye to bless ;
The far horizon like a sword’s edge shimmered,
Keen, gleaming, pitiless.
And all the long day through the hot air
quivered
Beneath the burning sky,
In dazzling dance of heat that dashed and
shivered.
It seemed as if hard by
The borders of this region, evil-favoured,
Life ended, Death began ;
But no; upon the plain a shadow wavered—
The shadow of a man.
What man was this by Fate or Folly driven
To cross the dreadful plain ?
A pilgrim poor ? or Ishmael unforgiven ?
The man was Andy Blane.
A stark old sinner, and a stout, as ever
Blue swag has carried through
That grim, wild land men name the Never-
Never,
Beyond the far Barcoo.
His strength was failing now, but his unfailing
Strong spirit still upbore
And drove him on with courage yet unquailing,
In spite of weakness sore.
When, 10, beside a clump of salt-bush lying,
All suddenly he found
A stranger, who, before his eyes, seemed dying
Of thirst—without a sound.
Some weave their web’of life well, and some
weave ill ;
One saves, one damns his soul,
But Andy was, for either good or evil,
A Man, complete and whole.
And so, beside the stranger on the sandy
Salt plain—a death-bed sad—

Eclipsed in death, was seen
No more, and in the spaces of the west, still
ruddy,
A star shone out serene.
As one, then, whom some memory beguiling
May gladden, too, and grieve,
The stranger, pointing up, said, sadly smiling,
“The Star of Christmas Eve 1 ”
Andy replied not. Unto him the sky was
All reeling stars —his breath
Came thick and fast—and life an empty lie was,
True one thing only—Death.
Beneath the moonlight; with the weird, wan
glitter
Of salt-bush all around,
He lay; but by his side in that dark, bitter,
Last hour, a friend he found.
Thank God !” he said, “ He’s acted more

And do just what you like best with the money,
In memory of me. ”
The stranger, smiling, with a little leaven
Of irony, said, “ Yea,
But there it shall not be. With me, in Heaven,
You’ll spend your Christmas Day.”
Then that old heathen, that grey back-block
stager,
Half-jestingly replied,
And laughed—and laughed again—’“Mate, it’s
a wager !”
And, grimly laughing, died.

VICTOR J. DALEY.
Beauty of Hilton’s Cove
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The lady is dead. The first families of the Point,
the Bay, the Cove and the Head remember her
well—the males, because a man has eyes for a
lovely woman, old or young; the women-folk,
because jealousy has memory. And, poor thing,
when all is told up, totalled and tallied, debited
and credited, God knows she was not happy
enough in her lot to be envded.
This is her story. Ike Hilton was an “old
hand,” and his wife was of the same sort. They
were both sent out, and Mrs. Hilton was confined
on the voyage. The circumstances of their trans-
portation need not be recalled, but as it is cus-
tomary to christen sea-born children after the
ship in which they first see light, Miss Hilton
was named Mary Ann Queen of Beauty, shortened
afterwards to Beauty Hilton. Here let me caution
Potts Pointers against double and treble names,
if one of them can be found in the list of convict
or even emigrant ships.
Hilton became rich, honestly or dishonestly;
honest people use the latter adverb, but, on the
contrary, others say otherwise. Naturally, Ike
and his missus died of rum. Rum at home caused
their exile, rum in Sydney freed them, rum made
them and their property, and rum killed them.
Hilton was a humorist in his way, and pretty
straight except for his crookedness. So he called
his home at Hilton’s Cove by the name of Tom-
ford House, alleging that he came from Paddle-
wyke, near Tomford, in Kent, His run on the
Bunkum Creek, near Bongowlali mine, he called
Paddlewyke Park, because it wasn’t a park; but
the Mortynes opposite had Gylone Park.
Before Mrs. Hilton (always called Mother
Hilton about the Bunkum) died, she and Beauty
lived a good deal at Paddlewyke, and Rollo
Mortyne was often a visitor. No doubt Beauty
was with his grand appearance, and
naturally fell in love with him. In her own simple
language she would have called it being “ shook ”
on him. Then she had a sneaking affection for
Tom Ray, the overseer, but hated his chum, the
storekeeper. The latter was quite a character.
No doubt he was a qualified surgeon, but whether
boundary-riding, burr-cutting or droving he did
not like being called Doctor, and just suffered his
mates to address him as Doc. Smiler, because he
oould not stop it,
I think it was in ’67 or ’6B that Beauty got her
smash. They were kangarooing; the three of
them, Ray, Doc. and Beauty. An “ old man ”
turned just as Beauty got up to him and down the
three came; the horse and kangaroo dead, and the
other next door to it.
That night Mrs. Hilton thanked the Lord, if
the prayed, that there was a surgeon ready to
tnend poor Beauty’s face: and that night Ray and
the doctor had a consultation. It seemed that
the overseer was trying to induce his chum to
test tome new treatment for the speedy recovery
of the—l was going to say girl—but I’ll call her
woman ; she was 27 at least.
Next day, Mr. Smiler asked the mother and
nurse to leave the room, whilst he dressed the face-
wounds, saying that he should have to extract
some splinters and earth, and would put the lady
under chloroform. He said the operation was
painless, but not pleasant to see. Miss Hilton
was conscious and consented (knowing that her
looks depended on good healing of the wounds)
to the withdrawal of her mother, if the servant
maid, who was a good nurse, would hold her
head.
It did not take long, and Beauty became, after
the plastic treatment of Doc, Smiler, just as ugly
as she had been lovely. Her mouth was twisted
and one eye drooped, and she had a grin—l can
only call it that—which was simply horrible.
There were no visits to Sydney now, and Rollo
Mortyne was told not to call again. Then Mr.
Ray married Beauty. It was no love-match on
either side; in a quiet way they lived comfortably
enough until the death of Mrs. or Mother Hilton.
But there was another death in the house—that of
the old doctor. Both Ray and his wife were at
his bedside when he had his final encounter with
a bad case—his own. He had become partially
paralysed, and was stuttering and gurgling:
“Another operation—Beauty again.” Ray put
this down to raving, but Mrs. Ray pondered.
Next year was that of the Bingiegong railway
accident. In one of the carriages, playing nap.
and winning, was a young squatter, who, in one
cut, lost all. His wife was called to the inquest,
and had, for a moment, to show her face.
Now, there was present a young surgeon called
Mortyne, brother of our Bunkum neighbour, who
showed more than ordinary civility to Mrs. Ray :
he was of the modern school, and plainly told her
that her face had been tampered with. Old Hilton
would have called it “faked.” And the great
surgeon, Mr. O’Kelly, from Sydney, was con-
sulted. With very little trouble, and a short
trip to Tasmania, our Beauty was restored.
It was a strangely-lovely classic face that
again' took Rollo Mortyne’s attention, and
it was now accompanied by a splendid
dot. You have all heard how the Mor-
tynes were dried and fried out of Queens-
land. It is even stated that after the banks had
foreclosed and sold them up,the rain came down,
when they were leaving Gylone, causing them to
get bogged in the horse-paddock. This second
marriage, too, was not so lucky or happy.
Mortyne heard about the temporary aberration of
comeliness from his brother, and even twitted
his wife with it. Of course, a woman who has
been a ghoul for a year or two knows how to be
nasty. Then Mortyne drank and died, leaving a
lovely widow and a son.
As I said, she died and that’s her story. Fifty
years hence her son, or grandson, Duncan
Mortyne, of Paddlewyke Park (which, doubtless,
hell change to Gylone Chase), will be making a
family tree. I bequeath him this history, for a
start. He will then very likely be Sir Duncan
Ray Mortyne, or, perhaps, my Lord Redeviva, of
Castle Turnford and Gylone Hall.

Two Showman’s Stories.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
"THE DAUGHTER OF A CLERGYMAN.”
Daisy Dome was very poor, very shabby,
very pretty, when she made her first application
for a situation to a theatrical manager. She must
have had histrionic talent, or this story would
never have been told.
Her manners were tender, nob to say alluring.
The manager felt inclined to engage her. The
manageress, when she heard that Daisy was “ the
daughter of a clergyman,” decided the point with
an emphatic affirmative.
The company took her measure variously. The
ladies held that she was “dangerous,” and
promptly ignored her. The men —the average
actor, in private life, is an intellectual pigmy
hearing her jabber scraps of Latin and French,
reverenced her as a prodigy of learning.
The leading man only expressed the lofty ambi-
guity—” Well, she knows how to kiss.”
The manager said she was “genteel;” the
manageress triumphantly recommended hat in the
words—“ She is a clergyman’s daughter.”
Hartley, the stage-manager, a widower with a
large family and small means, saw more of her
than anyone else, and summed her up enthusias-
tically—“ Exceptionally intellectual and a perfect
lady.”
Hartley spared neither time nor patience in
drilling her into “understanding” parts, taught
her all the “tricks” and “business” he knew,
and felt repaid for hours of drudgery by a tender
glance from her “ pathetic brown eyes.” Nobody
else ever found her eyes “ pathetic.” To Hartley
“ pathetic eyes ” were irresistible.
Daisy Dome was humbly and visibly grateful
for the stage-manager’s patience and encourage-
ment, and took childlike pains to please him.
* * *
Rehearsal was over. The company had
gladly cleared out of the stuffy premises. Hartley
was collecting books and turning out bunch-ligh ts,
when he caught sight of a disconsolate-looking
figure crouched in a corner.
“ Not gone home yet, little woman ! ”
“No, Mr. Hartley. It’s raining, and I have no
umbrella.”
“Let me get you one. There, don’t cry. Is
that all the trouble ? ”
“Oh, it’s very hard. We are so poor, dear
mamma and I. It’s so wretched. People sneer
at my shabbiness, and sometimes I feel like giving
up altogether. I want to get on. You have been
so good in helping me, but it is all so difficult, and
ray salary is so small.”
‘ ‘ Cheer up, my poor child. I have been through
it all. I know it all by heart. Don’t lose courage.
You are in a good theatre. You have youth,
beauty and talent; it’s only a question of time
and the world will be at your feet.”
“Oh, Mr. Hartley, it is so good of you to
encourage poor little me.”
“Now, look here, don’t be offended. If it’s
money that bothers you, let me help you. I’m
not well off,'but I’ve saved a little. I’m old
enough to be your father.”
“ You are nob old. Forty is a lovely age for a
man. ’’
Hartley was 56, wore a wig, and looked every
day 66. He was about to stammer out a confes-
sion when Daisy said, “ Don’t interrupt me, sir.
You are the best and dearest—oh, oh, what am I
saying ? Perhaps you wouldn’t mind taking me
home to dear mamma ?”
* * *
The manageress heard with kindly plea-
sure of the small legacy left to Daisy’s mamma
“ by a dear old college chum of poor father,” since
it enabled “the clergyman’s daughter ” to emerge
from, her chrysalis of shabbiness without a “ rise ”
in salary.
“ lb is a good thing you know how to dress, my
dear; it is. the first essential of a successful
actress.”
Hartley’s little hoardings decreased as Daisy’s
wardrobe expanded. At last came a day when
he, with shamefaced awkwardness, was obliged
to confess his inability to supply any more wants.
The brown eyes lost their pathetic tenderness.
“ I cannot have a new dress and I am to recite
at Bishopscourb before the best people ?”
The commonplaces of the scene that followed
may be easily understood. Hartley at last
pleaded the needs of his family, and that he was
in debt already.
“ If you care for your children best, why do you
want to marry me? I understand. It was a
speculation on your part. I was a profitable in-
vestment for your old age. lam to work to keep
you and your encumbrances. Since I now under-
stand your mercenary spirit, there is an end to
everything.”
“ Daisy ! ! ”
“ Please don’t make a scene. You are too old
for that sort of thing. A withered old sinner of
70! Pah!”
She stalked off like the real actress she was.
# « «
Hartley, after the first shock of his en-
lightenment, braced up his manhood, and vowed
to work all the harder for his family. “lb is
lucky I know the worst,” he said, self-consolingly.
If he had bub left it at that, it might have
been “the worst.”
* * •
The papers gave a glowing account of
Miss Daisy Dome’s success at Bishopscourb, and
one or two society scribblers described minutely
the charming toilette worn by her on the occasion.
“ Where could she have gob it?” thought Hart-
ley.
Soon she drove to rehearsals in a well-appointed
brougham.
“Dear father’s relations have relented,” she
told the manageress, who now began herself to see
an “ investment ”in “ the clergyman’s daughter.”
Hartley, who had heard from “dear mamma”
the whole family pedigree, listened to the news,
when it flittered to him, with icy gloom. As she
was leaving the theatre that afternoon, he drew
her gently into his office, and closed the door.
“ Why did you tell Mrs. Oarr-Burton that lie ?
Do you think you are deceiving me as well as the
others ? Daisy, you do nob know the end of all
this? For your own sake, don’t go to utter
ruin !”
She looked at him with cold blankness, and
then laughed hardly, while he shuddered.
“I had no idea you drank,” she said. “Try
and keep it from the management.”
Then she swept out, leaving him utterly as-
tonished.
. , . . .
Hartley stood in the manager’s room, a cheque
and open letter in his hand,
“I have been 15 years with you, Mr. Oarr-
Burton, and—this, without any reason ?”
“ Really, Hartley, now, we’re very sorry, bub,
after your disgraceful insult bo Miss Daisy Dome,
we had no alternative.”
“ Disgraceful insult ?”
“ There, there. Take the pledge, old man. In
consideration of your long and faithful service,
we have persuaded Miss Dome to keep it all
quiet. It’s really a police matter, you know, and
we’re risking a good deal on your behalf.”
Hartley lefb the room, completely dazed.
, * •
.. ~ ~ , ,
“ Can there be any doubt? said the
manager to the manageress. “ 1 would like to
believe old Hartley.”
“I would bake the word of an innocent child
like Daisy before that of fifty hardened old stagers
like Hartley. You forget, my dear, that she is
tha daughter of a clergyman I”
“OUT OF THE COMMON."
Old Bill Sclater was born “ unconven-
tional.” When he first saw light, it was found
that nature had equipped him with six toes to
each foot. As he grew up many offers were made
bo him by travelling menagerie-men to go on
salary as a “ freak,” bub his temperament was so
inordinately “unconventional” that ho declined
every temptation, so as to be “ out of the com-
mon.” The same abnormal twist in his nature
had led him to shine as a meteoric offender against
the law at various periods. I had heard of Bill,
but I didn’t know all this then.
Business was in a torpor, when I heard that
Bill was at Oakeyville, a remote mining town, and
I conceived the idea that a 12-toed man would fill
a then-existing gap in the show line.
After a 50-mile ride, I reached a place where
the track forked into two. A slab humpy, half-
concealed by a clump of trees, was visible, and I
rode over to make enquiries.
Against a doorpost leaned an angular youth,
dirty with a month’s abhorrence of water, his
head thatched with long, straight, fibrous hair,
From inside the hub came a continuous chaos of
hilarity.
“ Which is the road to Oakeyville 7 "
“That there one,” said the dirty angularity
with an unreadable shift of his head.
“ Which is ‘that there one?’”
“ You keep to the right ’’—savagely.
An inspiration !
“ S’pose you haven’t a drop of liquor handy 7”
The angularity raised his voice.
“I ain’t smelt grog,” he shouted, solemnly,
“for nigh on six munts, let alone basted it,”
A grizzled head and face appeared in the door-
way and was thrust over his right shoulder. After
an investigation of myself and trappings, the
grizzled head opened :
“Now, ain’t yer a awful liar, Boko7 What
the (polyglot gutturals) d’yer want to bluff the
young un for ? 'E ain’t no cop. Get off yer ’orse,
young feller, an’ jine me.”
I dropped: down without a second invitation and
entered the shanty, taking no notice of Boko’s
mnfflflrl nrnl«ia
muffled protests.
The grizzled man handed me a pannikin of rum.
“Now, yer don’t know me, do yer? No.
Well, I didn’t espect yer would. You arst these
coves who I am ; they know.”
“ These coves ” were three fairly good replicas
°f “Boko,” and were drunkenly sprawling on
?ne canvas stretcher. Two of them only grinned
knowingly. The third said—“ The old un’s a
fair daisy, be cripes I”
} Yer don t know me, but p r aps yer’ve ’eard
0
J, shook ray head.
. Dam queer I Ive been m quod fourteen
times, I have ; and broke out of quod five times,
You arst the copmen if they knows old Bill
Sclater, ’n’ see what they say.”
Here was luck ! I had tapped the vein with the
orst stroke of the pick. I drank a pannikin with
the daisy.” I slapped him on the back.
So you’re Bill Sclater? Why, I’ve heard of
Bill Sclater all over the country!”
“’Ave yer, young feller?”
“ Everywhere, s’w’elp me I”
“Now, what ’ave yer ’eard about me?”
“I’ve heard that you can carry more liquor to
the square inch than any other man in tha
(flaming) country.”
“Correct. Pub it there, young feller,” giving
me one of his huge paws.
• V 8 heard of you as a boss fighter, a boss
miner, an’ the smartest (crimson) cattle-duffer
that——”
“Rybuck! Put it there, young feller.”
“I’ve heard that you grow six toes on each
foot.”
“ By Cord ! young un; pub it there.”
“You’re the only man in the country that can
brag that, anyway,”
“You’re right, young feller. Bub it’s an un-
lucky brag. D’yer know what I’m a-goin’ ter
do?”
“ What ? "
“ Out two toes off, an’ then kill somebody,
That’ll be out o’ the common, won’t it? Them
(torrid) toes give me away to the copmen every
trip,”
“ By gum ! don’t you out ’em off, Bill.”
“ Why ? ”
“ Why ? Why, they’re worth a fortune to you.”
“ How ?”
“Go on show.”
“My ( —)!” roared Bill; as he
gripped me by the back of the neck with a hand
that felt like a spring-trap, and turned me slowly
round. His grisly form dilated, and he seemed
about to burst, while his heaving chest and throat
showed how sternly he was repressing himself.
At last, his voice came—hard, sarcastic, measured.
It even omitted the higher profanities,
“Yer damned skunk ! I’ve been waitin’ a hell
of a time for one of your sorb. When I let that
Italian bloke go who wanted me ter show my feet
ter women an’ kids, an’ offered ter pay me wages
for it, I swore it ’ud be the last I’d let off.”
“I was . . . only . . . joking , . .
Mister . . . S—S—Sclater 1” I gasped.
“lb don’t matter a curse if you was. I’m a-
goin’ to kill a man an’ it may as well be a blasted
showm ”
His voice died out suddenly. I remember no
more. The doctor says lam nob to ask questions
as to how I came to the hospital until I am quite
strong.

The Coolgardie gold boom is the only Australian boom for some years past
which shows signs of permanence. Coolgardie’s chief drawbacks are dust and heat
and myriads of flies, and the desert, variegated only by occasional
brackish mud-holes, which lies between it and the nearest civilisation. But the
gold is there, and gold is the one essential.
